I want to try Fedora core and Redhat's last*Free* distro.

What I want to know is if there are security issues to avoid with any
of the present distros or the older distros I'm interested in( Suse
8.1, Fedora Core, Redhat 9.1, etc) and I'd like to know which distro
the plug group has found to be:

a) easy to install
b) simple to work from both command and GUI
c) available as ISO
d) Useable as both server ware and desktop ware
e)Full featured
f) Free


<RANT ON>
Now, I don't mind paying for a distro that works.
But it goes along way with me if friends can get it for free.
I'm about done working with Linux if every time a distro gets to a
point it is usable to me, they go main street marketing route and
leave the little guys behind that helped them reach market share. I
may not be a developer that adds a significant piece of software to
the package, but I am the guinea pig that runs the dang thing and
finds flaws. I feel that for my contribution I should be compensated
by free access to the package. After all, I'm the one investing
hardware to run the distro on.
Besides, I'm a cheap old bastard.
<RANT OFF>

SO, any suggestions?

On Wed, 01 Dec 2004 20:01:06 GMT, techmage at aracnet.com
<techmage at aracnet.com> wrote:
> a) easy to install
> b) simple to work from both command and GUI
> c) available as ISO
> d) Useable as both server ware and desktop ware
> e)Full featured
> f) Free

This, and particulary your goals below, scream "Debian", at least in my opinion.

Debian is probably one of the least likely main-stream distros for
going comercial, ala Red Hat.  It's extremely easy to install Debian
with _no_ non-free software at all--this is the default. (adding
non-free is not hard either, and the install prompts you regarding
this).

I would not install debian from ISO though (although you can do this).
 The cd images are quite out of date, and packages are updated
frequently.  The biggest downside to debian is that its packaging
system really /wants/ broadband.  It will work on dialup, or
disconnected.  It's just not as slick, and your system will probably
be a little bit behind the times.

If you choose to go with debian, you will probably want to install the
"Sarge" release, which is one step closer to the bleeding edge than
the "stable" (woody) release.  (Stable, in debian terms, is
rock-solid, in my experience sarge is on par with redhat / mandrake
stable releases.)

-Rogan

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Dec  1 13:19:48 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 1 Dec 2004 13:19:48 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] QUick Reccomendation
In-Reply-To: <41b32f7a.7277844@mail.aracnet.com>
References: <41b11ff9.3308053@mail.aracnet.com>
	<c834ec804120112083f41f7be@mail.gmail.com>
	<477eba8c041201124682790b@mail.gmail.com>
	<41b32f7a.7277844@mail.aracnet.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412011305490.2943@pecos.madboa.com>

My installation experience is limited to Red Hat/Fedora, Debian, and 
Gentoo. I was just given responsibility for a SuSE box at work, but I 
didn't perform the installation.

Fedora is very easy to install. Fedora Core 3 has up-to-date 
software and security fixes usually arrive in a timely manner. The 
downside is the lifecycle of any given Fedora release is only 12 
months or so. I've never upgraded from one Fedora to another, so I 
can't really speak to the possibility of upgrading to each new 
release. You can use yum or apt or up2date to manage packages.

I haven't installed Debian in the past nine months, but I can't say it 
was easy. On a couple occasions, packages didn't upgrade cleanly, 
which required more Googling than I'd have liked. On the upside, it's 
very easy to keep your machine current using the "testing" release. 
It's also got a mind-boggling number of packages.

The Gentoo installation process is *very* well documented, but it 
assumes you know what you're doing and is rather time-consuming. I'm 
not a big fan of the manner in which the package-update process deals 
with config files, but otherwise it's an easy system to keep up to 
date with current software releases.

I've just started administering a SuSE box, but the yast2 tool makes 
package maintenance pretty easy. I'm not yet sold on using reiserfs as 
the default filesystem, but I can't say it's given me any troubles.

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

On Wed, 01 Dec 2004 14:03:56 -0800, Rich Burroughs <rich at paranoid.org> wrote:
> Bryan Murdock wrote:
> > I was just interested in some clarification on the current state of
> > installing Debian, I didn't mean to come off sounding like a troll.  I
> > apologize.
> I don't think you did, Bryan. And I don't think that someone can tell
> you what you can post in the thread and what you can't, just because
> they started it :)
> 
> I've had similar feelings about Debian, but it's been a while since I've
> tried the installer so perhaps things have gotten smoother.

The debian installer has certainly improved in the last 2-3 years, but
I must say my comments reflect the sarge installed, not the installer
packaged on the woody install ISOs.  Technically, this installer is
stil beta, however many people I know (my self included) have had good
luck with it.  It also provides 2.4 install kernels (use the bf24
kernel on the install medium--when you boot up there are
instructions.)

I've done at least one install where I did nothing but hit enter, and
had a useable system at the end--I wouldn't recommend this route, but
it can be done.  The installer is not graphical, however, which I
think scares a lot of people off.  Redhat, Mandrake, SuSe, and I'm
assuming Fedora Core have much fancier looking installers.

An additional dissadvantage to debian is that it will not
install/configure most recent video cards to do hardware acceleration
on it's own.  This comes back to the Free (as in speech) nature of the
distribution.  Recent ATI cards (9200 and newer?) and all GeForce
cards have proprietary drivers that are required for hardware
acceleration--thes can be installed, and the processes to do so are
well documented, but it is something you will have to do.  Mandrake
and others may be able to do this more automatically, but I don't have
experience with them.

In general, it is easier to get a "slick looking" linux install with a
distro like Mandrake etc.  but in my experience (most recently this
was RedHat 9 and Mandrake 10--so it's a bit dated) I was able to build
a more easily maintanable system with Debian, but I have dedicated
much more time to learning to use the debian tools and I am more
aquainted with the system, so I can't make an objective comparison.

-Rogan

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Dec  1 15:57:45 2004

On Wed, 1 Dec 2004, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> The reason this is relevant to Paul's comment is that I've been glad 
> that SuSE 9.1 installs reiserfs as a default because I haven't had 
> to sit through (whatever the Linux equivalent is for scandisk). But, 
> now I'm wondering if a bad spot on the drive is the problem, or if 
> there is something about reiserfs that I don't know about, and 
> should.

ext3, jfs, and xfs all solve the same problem; reiser isn't the only 
journaling filesystem available. :-)

> So, my question to Paul is, what are your hesitations about 
> reiserfs?

Early in both the 2.4 and 2.6 kernel-developement cycles, the kernel 
developers reported several problems with reiserfs. I'd guess that 
they've been cleaned up, but I don't follow filesystem development 
well enough to know. As recently as 2.4.18, some folks have 
experienced subtle problems with reiserfs on SMP systems.

That, and Zach Brown told me once that he thought Hans Reiser's code 
was little more than a mess. (Yes, that's an ad hominem argument, but 
I tend to trust Zach's judgment on system-level issues.)

But, like I said, reiserfs hasn't caused me any grief on the SuSE box, 
so I can't say I'm down on it; I just tend to use ext3 or xfs when 
given the choice.

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From eharrison at mail.mesd.k12.or.us  Wed Dec  1 15:58:28 2004
From: eharrison at mail.mesd.k12.or.us (Eric Harrison)
Date: Wed, 01 Dec 2004 15:58:28 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Debian Sarge SMP slowness?
Message-ID: <1101945508.20328.67.camel@ltsp.mesd.k12.or.us>


One of the school districts I often work with (who happen to be near
Redmond Washington ;-) is having SMP performance problems with the
Sarge 2.6.8-i686-smp kernel.

They are working on a backup server. The symptom is that if they fire
up 6 concurrent backup processes, every minute or so one of the
processes
goes to 100% wa state, the others go to 100% idle, the backup processes
go to a D state, and nothing happens for 10 - 60 seconds. Then
everything
works again for another minute.

Running the same tests on a Red Hat box, every process runs, raising
the load average to 6 as would be expected. The throughput is twice
as high as the Debian box.

Have any of you Debian gurus here seen this, and more importantly
know a fix? ;-)

-Eric

From pmvw at earthlink.net  Wed Dec  1 16:08:15 2004
From: pmvw at earthlink.net (Piet van Weel)
Date: Wed, 1 Dec 2004 16:08:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] QUick Reccomendation
In-Reply-To: <41b11ff9.3308053@mail.aracnet.com>
Message-ID: <E1CZeWr-0006RM-00@pop-a065d01.pas.sa.earthlink.net>

>> Okay, this is not intended to start a distro war.
>> I use Suse 9 and Redhat 7.3 and Suse 7.
>> I want to try Fedora core and Redhat's last*Free* distro.
>> 
>> What I want to know is if there are security issues to avoid with any
>> of the present distros or the older distros I'm interested in( Suse
>> 8.1, Fedora Core, Redhat 9.1, etc) and I'd like to know which distro
>> the plug group has found to be:
>> 
>> a) easy to install
>> b) simple to work from both command and GUI
>> c) available as ISO
>> d) Useable as both server ware and desktop ware
>> e) Full featured
>> f) Free

Ok... Having installed all of these... (at one time or another)
And basing my opinions on a freshly installed machine. (aka no mods)

a) Installation

SUSE: The initial install is REALLY easy. Even if you don't know linux.

Redhat and Fedora: Are about the same level of complexity. They seem to have
really adopted the ext3 file system. :(

Debian: After the initial set of menu's the installation goes pretty good.

Slackware: Recent slackware distro is actually harder to install then older
versions. Don't know why.

b) Command and GUI

Redhat and Fedora: This is the standard that everybody else gets graded by.

SUSE: From a GUI standpoint, SUSE did it right. You can transfer between
window/desktop managers and all your settings come with you. Very Nice.
Shell level. It all works, but SUSE has its own organization with regards to
the file system setup as well as some unique commands for configuration and
updates.

Debian: Matches up to Redhat just fine. Shell level nearly identical to 
Redhat. Major difference is all the apt commands.

Slackware: Various desktop mangagers can be frustrating; however, I find
slackware shell to be a tad faster than Redhat.

c) ISO

Redhat, Fedora, Slackware, SUSE, and Debian can all be obtained as an ISO. 
HOWEVER...

Redhat is getting out of distributing the ISOs.

SUSE is limiting the ISOs distributed.

Debian is best installed over the network.

d) Useable as both server ware and desktop ware

All of these distros can be used in either capacity.

e) Full featured

Fedora, Debian and Slackware: Definitely full featured. Downside is there
are several ISOs to download to get everything.

Redhat: Enterprise version has everything. ($179 -> $2500 depending on need)

SUSE: Professional version has everything. ($90)

f) Free

Ok. Ya want your free lunch... 

Debian, Fedora, and Slackware are all FREE. (as in air)

Redhat, well you saw the price.

SuSE. You saw it's price also.



I hope that this helps Harry. I didn't include distros like Mandrake, Yellow
Dog, Gentoo, Ubantu, SE-Linux or any of the myriad of others that exist. I
will say that the number of distros that have been reported lately is
considerably less then what there was several years ago.

The big players have their market share... and are beginning to extract rent
from us little people.

Piet van Weel

/bin/cat transport.lst | grep -vif no-imgate.lst > transport.map

grep -vif no-imgate.lst < transport.map > transport.map
cat transport.map | sort | grep -vif no-imgate.lst  > transport.map

      **********************************************************
          
                                 PRESENTATION

                               An Introduction
                                      to
                                  Subversion

                                      by

                                 Frank Torres
                           <frank.t326 at Comcast.net>


      Programmers (and other creative artists) need version control
      systems to coordinate different contributors' work and to keep 
      track of all the different releases or versions of a project.  
      In recent years, many people in the Open Source community have 
      used Concurrent Version System (cvs) for this.  cvs works well,
      but it has a number of problems and limitations.  

      Subversion (http://subversion.tigris.org/) is a replacement
      to cvs which has most of cvs's features without some of the
      problems and limitations of cvs.

I'm not sure if one's really expected to run those Debian kernels in
server production.  I mean, they're stable, but they're really
conservatively configured.

I (and I think every other serious Debian user) use make-kpkg on a tarball
from kernel.org.  This launches the normal kernel configurator (config,
menuconfig, whatever you choose) and then makes the kernel into a standard
Debian package that can be installed on any machine with 'dpkg -i'.

Nope, doesn't answer your question.  The best advice is to compare the
configs, of course, but two have suggested that already.  I think  you'll
find the Debian config to be, as I wrote, extremely conservative.

I might *almost* suggest Knoppix as a pathway to Debian.  There are
a few places, like apt configuration, where it is not QUITE properly
set up as an installable distro, but I imagine a couple of days of
attention from a Debian guru would fix this.   Knoppix is handy to
test hardware for compatability and do initial setup from the Live
CD, then finishing off with apt-get over broadband for server
applications and the like. 

But again, that would take some modification of the CD image, which
I am too lazy to do.  It might be easier to do with a Live DVD,
without trying to jam it all into a compressed CD.

One other issue, for newbies, is documentation (or the lack of it).
There are a lot of books, and a lot of websites, that answer questions
for Fedora, fewer for the other distros.  With Debian you will have
to do a lot more teaching and explaining - you can't just sling a
stack of books at them.  But since nobody reads the documentation
anyway, maybe this is not a big difference.

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Wed Dec  1 21:00:14 2004
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Wed, 01 Dec 2004 21:00:14 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Debian Sarge SMP slowness?
In-Reply-To: <20041202044734.GB10418@nellump.net>
References: <1101945508.20328.67.camel@ltsp.mesd.k12.or.us>
	<Pine.LNX.4.52.0412011908170.5902@shaft.bitmine.net>
	<20041202044734.GB10418@nellump.net>
Message-ID: <1101963619.8164.45.camel@dell.linux.box>

On Wed, 2004-12-01 at 20:47 -0800, Paul Mullen wrote:
> On Wed, Dec 01, 2004 at 07:11:37PM -0800, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> 
> > I (and I think every other serious Debian user) use make-kpkg on a
> > tarball from kernel.org.
> 
> Any particular reason not to use what the kernel-source packages
> provide (Debian-patched source trees, in other words)?

IMHO, distribution-patched kernels are wrong....

If you patch the kernel, it's with a patch that hasn't made it in to the
mainline kernel.  If it hasn't made it in to the mainline kernel, it
hasn't had as much testing or as many eyes looking at it.  As such, the
odds of a distribution-patched kernel being as stable and secure as a
vanilla kernel are, I think, lower.

-- 
Rob                                |  If not safe,
   Jabber: athlonrob at axpr.net   |    one can never be free.




From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec  1 21:12:03 2004
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 1 Dec 2004 21:12:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Recommended Wiki software?
Message-ID: <20041202051203.GB952@gate.kl-ic.com>


I have been using perl-based Kwiki-Kwiki for a few wikis on my sites.
One of the features of Kwiki is "AnonymousGnome" - this is how you 
are identified until you set your preferences (which puts a cookie
on your machine).  A lot of people forget, and so you find lots of
pages written by "AnonymousGnome" and no way to contact the author.

You also can get wiki spam, which takes some administrator editing
to completely remove from the system.

So ... I am wondering about alternative wiki implementations, 
preferably with a login and (flimsy) password, to remind people
to login and to raise the bar for wiki spam.  I realize that this
is not in the anarchic spirit of most wikis, but nobody says it has
to be your real name, right?

What alternatives are people familiar with?  I would prefer Perl-based,
as I understand that better than Python, but if it is easy to install
and fairly full-featured, I will consider almost anything.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Dec  1 21:18:09 2004

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412012102590.2943@pecos.madboa.com>

On Wed, 1 Dec 2004, AthlonRob wrote:

> If you patch the kernel, it's with a patch that hasn't made it in to 
> the mainline kernel.  If it hasn't made it in to the mainline 
> kernel, it hasn't had as much testing or as many eyes looking at it.

I wish this were true, but the fact is that Andrew Morton's -mm kernel 
series is the de facto testing ground for a large number of patches 
that make into mainline kernel. And I see that Alan Cox is once again 
releasing his -ac series. The -mm series is typically well tested, and 
I suspect the -ac series may once again regain that distinction if 
Alan rolls out releases consistently.

There's really too much kernel development for Linus to stay abreast 
of it all. He relies on folks like Andrew Morton to act as sort of 
Patch Lieutenants, who filter lots of code on its way to the mainline 
-- a path that can be slow, at times even glacial.

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

I have a situation where I need to grep -vif a file writing the results
back into the orginal file.  I'd _really_ rather not create a temporary to
do this and every trick I can think of doesn't work.

An example of the way I'm trying to avoid.

/bin/cat transport.lst | grep -vif no-imgate.lst > transport.map

With "relay_recipients.map" containing the final results.  I don't want to
create the "transport.lst" if at all possible.  Keep the hits on the disk
to a minimum.

Tricks like these _don't_ work.

grep -vif no-imgate.lst < transport.map > transport.map
cat transport.map | sort | grep -vif no-imgate.lst  > transport.map
cat transport.map | sort | grep -vif no-imgate.lst | sort > transport.map

I realize as I write this I was hoping I'd get some MS/PC-DOS like
behavior.

Grep has no option for "inplace editing".  In the back of my mind a
thought floats around that I saw something many years ago (real years not
Internet years :-) about how to do this.  I just can't nail it down.

Any ideas?


Thanks,
Rod

> I have a situation where I need to grep -vif a file writing the results

Why?  Is there not enough space?  The performance difference should be
negligible, and debugging and maintaining the script is *far* easier
when you can re-run it against the same file.

That said, if the file is small enough to fit in memory, you should be
able to do a "cat file1 | grep > file1".

Work safely,
  Aaron

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Dec  1 23:27:06 2004
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 1 Dec 2004 23:27:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Running a shell script?
In-Reply-To: <200412012327.21037.guy1656@ados.com>
References: <200412012327.21037.guy1656@ados.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec8041201232739360ad2@mail.gmail.com>

On Wed, 1 Dec 2004 23:27:21 -0800, GLL <guy1656 at ados.com> wrote:
> 
> OK I just d/l'ed "jre-1_5_0-linux-i586-rpm.bin"
> I am trying to install it so I can get Azureus running.
> So, how do I 'run' this?
> 
> Since rpms can (with luck) be installed with a double-click,
> why are these packages created as [yourfilename].rpm.bin?
> Is there any reason why the developers have to sabotage it so it can't install
> with a double-click?

It's apparently not an rpm.  The "rpm" in the name probably means it
makes use of features of an rpm-based system to install itsself. 
Filenames like this typically specify the OS, architecture, and system
information that this particular binary is built for.  In this case, 
an i586 machine running linux, whith rpm (so, probably Redhat,
Mandrake, or Fedora).
If you are running Debian for example, you may be better off with a
different package.  And if you're on some other architecture (such as
a mac) you'll need a different binary.

Most .bin files are binary executeables.  You can run "file
jre-1_5_0-linux-i586-rpm.bin" to confirm this, it may be a shell
script.  In either case, running: (at a terminal)

chmod a+x jre-1_5_0-linux-i586-rpm.bin
./jre-1_5_0-linux-i586-rpm.bin

should do it for you.

Or use a gui tool to set the executable bit, and then you can probably
double-click it.

-Rogan

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Thu Dec  2 00:42:50 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Thu, 02 Dec 2004 00:42:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: [Q] shell scripting
References: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412011755330.17340-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
Message-ID: <pan.2004.12.02.08.42.47.845876@nakedape.cc>

On Wed, 01 Dec 2004 18:08:52 -0800, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> Grep has no option for "inplace editing".  In the back of my mind a
> thought floats around that I saw something many years ago (real years not
> Internet years :-) about how to do this.  I just can't nail it down.

No, but sed does.  If you want to remove lines matching a pattern, try
this:

sed -i -e '/PATTERN/d'

Of course, since you've got a list of patterns to match in
'no-imgate.lst', you'd probably rather use that than write out all the
patterns.  Something like this:

ARG=" -i "
for l in $(cat no-imgate.lst); do
  ARG="$ARG -e '/$l/d'"
done

sed $ARG transport.map

Wil

Wil Cooley                                 wcooley at nakedape.cc
Naked Ape Consulting                        http://nakedape.cc

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Dec  2 06:44:23 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 2 Dec 2004 06:44:23 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Running a shell script?
In-Reply-To: <200412012327.21037.guy1656@ados.com>
References: <200412012327.21037.guy1656@ados.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412020640580.3057@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>

On Wed, 1 Dec 2004, GLL wrote:

> OK I just d/l'ed "jre-1_5_0-linux-i586-rpm.bin" I am trying to 
> install it so I can get Azureus running. So, how do I 'run' this?

chmod +x jre-*.bin
./jre-*.bin
# agree to license
rpm -Uvh jre-*.rpm

> Since rpms can (with luck) be installed with a double-click, why are 
> these packages created as [yourfilename].rpm.bin? Is there any 
> reason why the developers have to sabotage it so it can't install 
> with a double-click?

The rpm philosophy is based on the idea of 100% hands-off 
installations -- which doesn't work with software that comes with a 
click-through license. Hence the .bin file: it's essentially a license 
wrapper around the rpm.

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

From Steve.Daggett at fiserv.com  Thu Dec  2 09:46:35 2004
From: Steve.Daggett at fiserv.com (Daggett, Steve)
Date: Thu, 2 Dec 2004 09:46:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] [Q] shell scripting
Message-ID: <008A26A81DAD96489E7D4FCF259849982D4162@eft01ms01.eft.fiserv.net>

 
Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> 
> I have a situation where I need to grep -vif a file writing 
> the results back into the orginal file.  I'd _really_ rather 
> not create a temporary to do this and every trick I can think 
> of doesn't work.

<<SNIP>>
> 
> Any ideas? 

Writing the script in Perl would be a good idea. 
 
  BASH does support a memory array option.  I've used arrays in the past to
process large chunks of data without taking a disk hit.  

Advanced Bash-Scripting Guide: Chapter 26. Arrays
http://www.tldp.org/LDP/abs/html/arrays.html

  Another old server admin hack would be to create a RAM disk to hold your
temporary files.  Your system will still take a I/O hit when you access the
temp file, but it won't actually spin up the disk.  

Steve D...

Here's a puzzler:

I just updated the clock on a router (an OpenWRT'd Linksys WRT54G) and
apparently blew away a connection I had open through the router's
masquerading.  I am pretty sure that when I updated the clock (about 4
years) I caused the conntrack information to get expired, and so even
though both ends were still there and trying to talk, the router
didn't know how to connect them.  Does anyone know a way to poke the
conntrack information back into the conntrack table?  It would be a
bit of a pain, but by reconnecting to both the hosts, I think I could
determine the ports involved.

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Thu Dec  2 16:35:35 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Thu, 02 Dec 2004 16:35:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: dump with multiple mount points
References: <20041203001711.GC15239@dalsemi.com>
Message-ID: <pan.2004.12.03.00.35.34.642191@nakedape.cc>

On Thu, 02 Dec 2004 16:17:11 -0800, Colin Kuskie wrote:

> How does one use dump to create a tape backup with multiple
> mountpoints?
> 
> I ask because I tried this, which seemed obvious, but is wrong:
> 
> dump /
> dump /usr
> dump /home
> 
> and I ended up with a just a backup of /home.

Use /dev/nstX instead of /dev/stX.  nstX is the non-rewinding tape
interface.  stX rewinds automatically when it's closed.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley                                 wcooley at nakedape.cc
Naked Ape Consulting                        http://nakedape.cc
* * * * Linux, UNIX, Networking and Security Solutions * * * *

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Dec  3 17:06:09 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 3 Dec 2004 17:06:09 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Multiple DVD writing
In-Reply-To: <20041203200003.BB27B599A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200412040106.iB41692K030538@m5p.com>

>From: Jim Karlock <Jkarlock at earthlink.net>
> Does anyone know of software that will allow the simultaneous writing to 
> multiple DVD burners?
> 
> Ideally, there would be one player, a hard disk and 3-10 writers (8x 
> speed), so that I could make up to 10 copies at once. Copies could be made 
> from the player or hard drive. Hopefully using only off the shelf PC hardware.

I haven't heard of any such software, but the simple method of running
3-10 cdrecord/growisofs processes would likely be simplest. As long as
you fire them all off close together (say within 10 seconds), the system
will take care of caching the source and avoid chewing the hard disk. The
problem will be getting a system with that number of controllers.

	<5.1.0.14.2.20041201140100.01c1afc0@www.hotsushi.com>
	<5.1.0.14.2.20041201143312.00ac40d0@www.hotsushi.com>
	<4.2.2.20041202230810.041e3ab0@mail.earthlink.net>
Message-ID: <1658.4.15.119.130.1102125613.squirrel@4.15.119.130>

> Does anyone know of software that will allow the simultaneous writing to
> multiple DVD burners?
>
> Ideally, there would be one player, a hard disk and 3-10 writers (8x
> speed), so that I could make up to 10 copies at once. Copies could be made
> from the player or hard drive. Hopefully using only off the shelf PC
> hardware.

You'd need additional IDE controllers (not too expensive)... but I'm not
sure if the PCI bus would be able to handle all that.  You might have to
go the 64-bit PCI route to move that much data.

I'm not sure it will be a problem, but I could see it causing issues...
especially with that much data being moved.

Rob

#RSAAuthentication yes

AuthorizedKeysFile      .ssh/authorized_keys

HostKey /etc/ssh/ssh_host_dsa_key

PasswordAuthentication yes
PermitEmptyPasswords no

   In my ~/.ssh/authorized_keys file (on the target server/workstation) I
have entries for the other three hosts in linex that begin "ssh-dss ..."

   My questions are:

   1) Is the problem on the remote notebook host or the target
server/workstation host?

   2) Is the problem a missing public or private key on one side or the
other?

   3) How can I restore the passphrase request without rerunning ssh-keygen
on all hosts?

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Dec  4 09:24:21 2004
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 4 Dec 2004 09:24:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] A server that isn't
Message-ID: <20041204172421.GA20636@gate.kl-ic.com>


I would like to design a backup server that is extra secure.  The way
I plan to set things up, the server rsyncs to the backup clients and
pulls information from them, but other than that server-internally-
initiated process, the server does not respond to any externally-
initiated service requests.  If you ran nmap on the server, you would
not see any open ports.  iptables on the server would continue
internally initiated connections, but would not respond to externally
initiated ones.  

With one semi-exception - the backup server would respond to pings.
So in a way, the server is not really a server.  Is there another 
name for this kind of "silent server"?  (submarine?)

The idea is to not worry about securing any services on the backup
server, because none are offered.  The fewer server-side programs 
exposing their security holes to the network, the better.  Especially
on a backup server, which has copies of all the treasures of all
the clients.

So how does the backup server respond to restore requests?  By polling;
it goes out to the client machines and looks for restore requests, on
a fairly frequent basis.  It will only restore files to the particular
client it got them from (using the same ssh authentication used for
backup), to a separate user owned directory (permission 700) and only
if "restore permissions" on the backup server permit it (otherwise it
"restores" a "not-permitted" message).  Some security-related restores
will require sysadmin participation.

I figure a polling interval of once per minute should be adequate;
it will take longer than that to construct a list of available files
on the backup server, so think "hour-long" process rather than
"quick response".  Some restores will need sysadmin attention, anyway;
the restore media may be off-line, in storage.  A faster alternative 
might be "ping-initiated polling", where the backup server senses that
it is being pinged by a client and responds immediately with a poll. 
However, this alternative method seems insecure to things like Denial
of Service attacks.

But all this is de-novo speculation;  there are probably already
standard ways to do what I am planning.  Perhaps my caution is
misplaced; if I am relying on server-outbound rsync and ssh, then
maybe server-inbound ssh adds no new insecurities.  Any comments?

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From heinlein at madboa.com  Sat Dec  4 09:48:58 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sat, 4 Dec 2004 09:48:58 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] SSH2: Restoring Passphrase Request
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412040839510.17285@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412040839510.17285@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412040941290.6488@pecos.madboa.com>

On Sat, 4 Dec 2004, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  I use ssh2 rather than telnet to connect among machines. When 
> connecting from my notebook to my server/workstation I am no longer 
> asked first for my passphrase, but only for my password on the 
> remote machine.

It may be that, on either machine, your ~/.ssh directory and its files 
are group- or world-readable, which will cause ssh or sshd to ignore 
their contents.

If that's not the case, then, on the server, fire up sshd in 
test/debug mode on an alternate port:

   [root at server]# sshd -d -p 222

Then contact it from your notebook, also in debug mode:

   [rshepard at notebook]$ ssh -v -p 222 server

The problem ought to show up in one of the terminal sessions. If you 
can't spot it in your scrollback buffer, you might try launching the 
sessions using script(1) and then reviewing the resulting typescript 
files.

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

> What I want to know is if there are security issues to avoid with any

I just want to give some added feedback on this. I just installed Fedora 
for the first time (Fedora Core 3) and I was really impressed by the 
ease of installation. It autodetected all of my hardware correctly, the 
only thing I needed to do to configure X was pick a resolution.

I chose to use their default Gnome setup, and there was an icon right 
there in the top bar warning me that there were updates I needed to 
make. I ran up2date and it had to grab about 20-25 packages, but it went 
very smoothly, including upgrading the kernel RPM.

I think it was the smoothest Linux install I've ever done.

With the updates available that easily and the inclusion of SELinux, it 
seems good from a security standpoint as well, although I haven't poked 
at it much yet.

As a side note, I'm pretty amazed at how easy the installs have gotten 
in general. I'm sure that a lot of people take that for granted, it's 
what they expect out of an operating system, but when I think back to 
the old days of downloading floppy images, screwing around with X 
configs, and all of that, it's really something to see the progress that 
has been made.


Rich

From rich at paranoid.org  Sat Dec  4 12:40:39 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Sat, 04 Dec 2004 12:40:39 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] A server that isn't
In-Reply-To: <B0002967557@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0002967557@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <41B220C7.6050704@paranoid.org>

Charles Sliger wrote:
> I haven't heard of a backup server like this, but it certainly could be
> done.
> Why the DOS attack concerns?  Is this system on the internet?

It sounds like it. That would be an alternate strategy, to set up a 
second, private network that the backups happen over. That would work if 
the boxes are all on the same network.

Keith, I can understand the paranoia about services running, you never 
want to do that if you don't need them, but in this kind of situation I 
would be more likely to run sshd on the backup server and just rsync 
through that from the clients when needed, rather than have all that 
polling going on. Yes, it's a service running that is a potential source 
of danger, but there haven't been many big problems with OpenSSH. I 
think that code is being scrutinized pretty closely, and if a problem is 
found you know there will be a patch coming quickly.

And as I think you hint at in your original email, the clients will have 
sshd running in the plan you propose, so if a hole in it crops up then 
the data is still vulnerable on the client boxes themselves. It's a 
problem you'll need to deal with in any case.

You could configure sshd so it only accepts connections from the client 
IPs, and set up redundant iptables rules to do the same things. I think 
that's a pretty secure setup.

One of the books I read a long time ago used the metaphor of a seesaw, 
with security on one end and convenience on the other. You always need 
to strike that balance based on your own situation, and the value of the 
data you need to protect.


Rich

On Fri, 03 Dec 2004 22:12:51 -0800, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 
> Aha! It never occurred to me that someone local would use a domain from
> off shore. But, why not? The web doesn't care, so why should I?

The Cocos Islands is one of those tiny countries that decided to
capitalize on having its own gTLD during the Internet Gold Rush, as did
Tuvalu (.tv), Tongo (.to) and Micronesia (.fm), because as gTLDs they made
a bit of sense (to English speakers, at least).  I was, of course, too
late in trying to register to get the .com, .net, and .org.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Dec  4 15:36:52 2004
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Sat, 4 Dec 2004 15:36:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] A server that isn't
In-Reply-To: <20041204172421.GA20636@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20041204172421.GA20636@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20041204233652.GK27619@bavariati.org>

On Sat, Dec 04, 2004 at 09:24:21AM -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
[snipped: a polling backup server cloaked in mystery]
> But all this is de-novo speculation;  there are probably already
> standard ways to do what I am planning.  Perhaps my caution is
> misplaced; if I am relying on server-outbound rsync and ssh, then
> maybe server-inbound ssh adds no new insecurities.  Any comments?

I think your design is very interesting.  And not just in a Howard
Hughes germ-phobia kind of way.  But leaving yourself no way to nudge
the server if it nods off, or even maintain it serves as a sure
invitation for ol' Murphy's Law to have its way with it.
At, of course, the worst possible time.

SSH is pretty darn secure, especially if you disable passwords, use
DSA keys and restrict most keys to specific tasks and IP addresses.

And then you can have something with a simple, ordinary, maintainable
design that even your typical hung-over sleep-deprived self-taught
half-assed sysadmin can maintain and diagnose. (/me raises hand)

Bruce Schneier talks about balancing security against other factors;
security takes away from other things like functionality,
maintainability, reliability and simplicity.  So one looks for the
sweet spot while exploring alternatives (such as your backup system.)

-- 
"These people program the way Victorians dress.  It takes two hours
and three assistants to put on your clothes, and you have to change
before dinner.  But everything is modular."    -- Miles Nordin, on PAM

/dev/hda1     /boot       92.0 MB Linux native

From dvhoward at teleport.com  Sun Dec  5 00:11:43 2004
From: dvhoward at teleport.com (Douglas Howard)
Date: Sun, 5 Dec 2004 00:11:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] System hangs
References: <200412041713.27408.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <001801c4daa2$12c39060$542179a5@Dell2650>

>From time to time my system hangs. It's usually when I have an xterm window
>open, along with KMail, and Patience, and then I try to open something 
>else,
>like K3b. When I reset the machine and try running K3b (to burn CD's) as 
>the
>only program I start myself, it works fine.

Failing on high demand can indicate RAM  failure.
Run memtest86.
The knoppix CD boots with this program as an option.

Just in case it's temperature, at reboot, go to your BIOS and examine CPU 
temp and fan speed.

In case it's static, put a note on your keyboard to always touch your PC 
metal case
or something grounded before touching keyboard or mouse.
Problem may go away.
Static gets to motherboards via these paths and locks up CPU.


>The system has 192 Mb of RAM (free -m says 186). It has been suggested to 
>me
>that the problem might be a bad spot on the hard drive. I have begun 
>testing,
>but now need advice on how to proceed.

Swapfile may be too small. Check the needs of your distribution for a 
server.
Swapfile takes a while to corrupt itself.
Run badblocks on swapfile too.
Do  swap off   first or assign another partition as swap.

>Details:
>The partitioning looks like this, according to the lvm tool in YaST2:
>/dev/hda1     /boot       92.0 MB Linux native
>/dev/hda5      swap     180.0 MB Linux swap

>I have run badblocks on the three ext2 partitions, hda7, hda8, and hda9, 
>and
>found no problems. Now I'm thinking about running badblocks on other
>partitions. I understand that a partition should not be mounted when
>badblocks is run, so I'm trying to figure out how to go about testing the
>swap partition. Should I make use of some of the old ext2 space and create
>another swap partition before proceeding?

On Sun, 5 Dec 2004, Dave Phillips wrote:
> I am wanting to dive into the world of debian and am looking for a good
> guide.  I have consulted the debian manual and can get the basics up and
> running, but am looking for a "where to go from here" guide.  I am
> pretty familiar with gentoo (love everything about it but the long
> compile times!) but from what I noticed (i think) with debians packaging
> system is I was able to install kde for instance and it didnt install X
> along with it.  I am probably doing something wrong.

No, you probably didn't do anything wrong.  KDE doesn't depend on an X
server (though xlibs were surely installed).  After all, your KDE-based
clients could run on a remote server.

There's a meta-package called x-window-system (I think) that should be
what you need.

As for a "guide", well, I don't know.  I just browse the repository and
get a feel for how it's structured and how the packages are divided up.

You might be taking the manufacturer's ratings too seriously; a 600 or 650
VA unit should be able to handle three or four systems without problems. 
I wouldn't necessarily expect to an hour of runtime out of it, but it
should be adequate to survive a brown-out or shutdown in case of a
black-out.  My experience has been that the manufacturer's specs are for
bigger budgets than I generally work with.

I've found the Debian Reference to be handy.

  apt-get install debian-reference-en

(Assuming your preferred language is English. They have many other
translations available, too.)

Once installed, it can be read from a web browser using the URL:
  file:///usr/share/doc/Debian/reference/index.html

From DVHoward at teleport.com  Mon Dec  6 13:40:48 2004
From: DVHoward at teleport.com (Douglas Howard)
Date: Mon, 6 Dec 2004 13:40:48 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] System hangs
References: <200412041713.27408.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <019301c4dbdc$46fdc220$512279a5@k61800DVH>

>The system has 192 Mb of RAM (free -m says 186). 
>Details:
>The partitioning looks like this, according to the lvm tool in YaST2:
>/dev/hda1     /boot       92.0 MB Linux native
>/dev/hda5      swap     180.0 MB Linux swap

>I have run badblocks on the three ext2 partitions, hda7, hda8, and hda9, 
>and
>found no problems. Now I'm thinking about running badblocks on other
>partitions. I understand that a partition should not be mounted when
>badblocks is run, so I'm trying to figure out how to go about testing the
>swap partition. Should I make use of some of the old ext2 space and create
>another swap partition before proceeding?

1. The swap file appears to be too small. This link describes swap space:
http://linux.about.com/od/linux101/l/blnewbie4_2_13.htm  
*Note that swapfile size is usually 1.5 to 2x the RAM size.*

Googling on: linux swapfile size 
will get you more info.

2. You seem to need info on partitions so you can manage them yourself.
Google on: fdisk parted linux magic druid cfdisk sfdisk 
to find many good links on partition management.
This link is informative:
http://www.linux.com/howtos/Multi-Disk-HOWTO-12.shtml 

3. Automated installation programs will do unpredictable things with partitions
such as using existing ones or not using them, or wiping them all out and 
repartitioning the drive in its own way. You may have such a situation too that
you will want to clean up.
The mount command will tell you what logical drives/partitions are mounted for use.



From rich at paranoid.org  Mon Dec  6 15:21:45 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Mon, 6 Dec 2004 15:21:45 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] System hangs
In-Reply-To: <019301c4dbdc$46fdc220$512279a5@k61800DVH>

Message-ID: <62355.24.21.168.187.1102375305.squirrel@www.paranoid.org>

Douglas Howard wrote:
> 1. The swap file appears to be too small. This link describes swap space:
> http://linux.about.com/od/linux101/l/blnewbie4_2_13.htm
> *Note that swapfile size is usually 1.5 to 2x the RAM size.*

I hadn't noticed that in his original email, but you're right. I thought
the Fedora install warns you when you try to use a swap partition that's
smaller than the amount of memory?

To the original poster, I know there's a section in the old RH manual
about adding swap, it's here:

http://www.redhat.com/docs/manuals/linux/RHL-8.0-Manual/custom-guide/s1-swap-adding.html

It all applies to Fedora too. If you've made a swap partition that's too
small you might just want to add a swap file. Those instructions are at
the bottom.

I'm not saying this is the cause of the problem you described, but it's
good to have enough swap in any case...


Rich

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Dec  7 13:46:03 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 7 Dec 2004 13:46:03 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Fedora Core 3
In-Reply-To: <41b61f3e.13066203@mail.aracnet.com>
References: <41b61f3e.13066203@mail.aracnet.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071339450.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>

On Tue, 7 Dec 2004 techmage at aracnet.com wrote:

> Right now I have a wonderful install of Fedora Core 3 on the box and 
> everything appears to work except the internet connection.
>
> KPPP dials in and connects but after that, nothing. No browsing, No 
> email, No traceroute, no pings off the local network. I'm not sure 
> why, but that's why I'm asking you all.

Run /sbin/ifconfig and see if you've got a ppp interface with what 
appears to be a valid Internet address.

-> If so, a) does /etc/resolv.conf look kosher? b) what does "netstat
    -rn" show? can you ping the Gateway address? c) does running
    "/etc/init.d/iptables stop" help?

-> If not, look in /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ and see if there's
    a file called ifcfg-ppp (or something very similar).

    -> If so, could you post the contents (remove any sensitive info)?

    -> If not, try using /usr/bin/system-config-network to configure
       your interface.

Ugh. There are too many if-then clauses in the troubleshooting 
steps...

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Dec  7 15:06:09 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 7 Dec 2004 15:06:09 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Fedora Core 3
In-Reply-To: <41B62EB3.7020604@paranoid.org>
References: <41b61f3e.13066203@mail.aracnet.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071339450.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>
	<41B626F8.1040905@aracnet.com> <41B62EB3.7020604@paranoid.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071450250.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>

On Tue, 7 Dec 2004, Rich Burroughs wrote:

> Harry Howard wrote:
>
>>  Okay, I got this fixed by ifdown my nic and then dialing in, this 
>>  allowed me to setup/get email and browse. Now the question is, 
>>  what needs a fix so I can leave my nic up and dial in?
>
> It's hard to say, it depends on what's causing the problem. I've 
> never had to disable a NIC to have KPPP work. It's possible that 
> it's something with the gateway, if you have a default gateway set 
> up in the settings for the NIC.

If I had to guess, I'd say that Rich hit the nail on the head. I 
haven't run a PPP interface in a looonnng time, but I bet your eth0 
interface remains the default gateway even after your ppp interface is 
activate.

To see if that's true,

1. Get your system into it's normal "just booted" state and run
    "/bin/netstat -rn" (or /sbin/route without arguments; they're
    functionally equivalent).

    Take note of any line that has a destination of 0.0.0.0. The second
    column ("Gateway") should display the route that sits between you
    and any non-local IP addresses.

2. Use kppp to dial your ISP. Once it connects, run "netstat -rn"
    again. If things are working correctly, the Gateway will have
    changed. If not, then a) the Gateway hasn't changed or b) there is
    no default gateway.

If the route has NOT changed, then try to see if any of your network 
settings set a static gateway device:

   grep GATEWAY \
     /etc/sysconfig/network \
     /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ifcfg-*

Note: /usr/share/doc/initscripts-*/sysconfig.txt describes in detail 
the options available to the files in /etc/sysconfig.

If there's a hard-coded GATEWAY or GATEWAYDEV in there somewhere, 
you'll probably need to get rid of it. Doing so might mean networking 
will be broken the next time you [re]plumb your interfaces, so be 
careful. :-)

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From rich at paranoid.org  Tue Dec  7 15:25:31 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Tue, 07 Dec 2004 15:25:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Fedora Core 3
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071450250.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>
References: <41b61f3e.13066203@mail.aracnet.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071339450.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>	<41B626F8.1040905@aracnet.com>
	<41B62EB3.7020604@paranoid.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071450250.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>
Message-ID: <41B63BEB.60205@paranoid.org>

Paul Heinlein wrote:

> If there's a hard-coded GATEWAY or GATEWAYDEV in there somewhere, 
> you'll probably need to get rid of it. Doing so might mean networking 
> will be broken the next time you [re]plumb your interfaces, so be 
> careful. :-)

Yeah, the real issue is how the box is networked. Is the ethernet card 
just for a local network, like private address space, and the PPP 
connection is to connect to the Internet? If so, then you don't actually 
need a default gateway associated with the NIC.

The default gateway is used to route packets destined for addresses that 
do not have a direct route already in the routing table. If the ethernet 
is just used for your local subnet, then there should already be a route 
in the routing table for that. It doesn't need to use the default 
gateway. The PPP connection, on the other hand, will use it a lot, for 
any traffic destined for the Internet.

My guess is that there is a default gateway configured for the NIC and 
that the PPP connection is not able to set a new one. If your setup is 
like I describe above, then the solution will be to remove the gateway 
for the NIC. That's probably what the system scripts did for you 
automatically when you shut down the interface.

I've just started using Fedora so I'm not sure, but there should be an 
easy GUI way to do that part. There should be some kind of network 
configuration tool in their Gnome menu. I'm in Windows right now, sorry, 
I can't be more specific... :)


Rich

If I may be so immodest:

Burning CDs on Linux
http://www-128.ibm.com/developerworks/linux/library/l-cdburn.html

mkisofs -o test.iso -rv  V disk_label  /files-to-be-copied
cdrecord -v -eject speed=8 dev=0,1,0 test.iso 

It's an old article that does not cover the 2.6 kernel, but the mkisofs 
commands are the same.

server went to reboot without it, it would just hang until the "key" was 
put in.
It'd be interesting to play with and find out.

Harry


From rich at paranoid.org  Tue Dec  7 18:07:15 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Tue, 07 Dec 2004 18:07:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Fedora Core 3
In-Reply-To: <41B652A8.8060801@aracnet.com>
References: <41b61f3e.13066203@mail.aracnet.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0412071339450.13540@glenfiddich.cse.ogi.edu>
	<41B652A8.8060801@aracnet.com>
Message-ID: <41B661D3.9040208@paranoid.org>

Harry Howard wrote:

> Okay, it was a defined default gateway in one of the configuration files 
> in sysconfig.
> Once I set it to 0.0.0.0 and rebooted, all was Kosher, as they say.

Cool, I'm glad you found a way to make it work :)

A slightly cleaner way would be to leave the entry blank, like:

GATEWAY=""

Or just comment the line out.

That way you're not setting a gateway at all for that device, as opposed 
to setting one of 0.0.0.0. When Paul mentioned that address it was 
because that's the destination that gets set for the gateway, it 
represents all other addresses not specified in the routing table.

 From a pratical standpoint it probably doesn't matter, it sounds like 
it's ok as it is :)


Rich

I think some (read: a rare few) recent (post-2002) boards can do it.  It 
would not surprise me at all to see this become a more common, possibly 
ubiquitous feature in the future as portable drive bays with USB and 
Firewire connections (allowing you to use a standard ATA or SATA disk 
as a portable disk), NVRAM USB keychains, and bajillion-in-one portable 
media readers become dirt cheap and ultra-ubiquitous and finally kill 
off the floppy dinosaur and slow-ass, relatively expensive CDRW and 
DVDRW markets.

> that a  server would need it to boot, sort of a security key. That 
> way, if the server went to reboot without it, it would just hang until 
> the "key" was put in.   

Get a USB-bootable board and make it so.

From raanders at acm.org  Tue Dec  7 20:38:45 2004
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Tue, 7 Dec 2004 20:38:45 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Burn some directories to a CD
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412071202410.22092-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412072030300.24315-100000@main.cyber-office.net>

Rich, Carla.  Thanks for the input.  I've been there are done all that.  
The issue was I had two directories and their files and the standard 
mkisofs flattens them into one.  One directory has a links (with the 
same name) to files in the other.  Mkisofs doesn't like that too much.  I 
was also fighting the -R which kept smashing my very nice files names into 
8.3s.  Not very useful.

I did almost come up with a solution by dropping the -R butthere is no way 
to get -- that I can find -- two or more directories with their structure 
from a single command line call of mkisofs.

FWIW, this is for a bunch of Postfix servers and the configuration and 
scripts are almost identical.  It would be very nice to pull all the stuff 
off one and copy onto the others plus have a backup.  So I finally decided 
to scp to one system with a directory structure that takes into account 
each server and then create the CDROM from that.

Again thanks for the input.


Rod

> I''ve had a pretty experience with DSL as a bootable CD. I know that its 
> USB pendrive booting is fairly documented .  Supposedly feather linux

I've spent a good portion of the 24 hours researching these things...

It turns out I made an incorrect assumption.  I assumed most of these
LiveCDs would be full of useful utilities to work on a system that
wouldn't otherwise boot.  Strangely enough, they're almost *all*
targetting traditional Desktop users as a full Desktop Distro On The Go.

Most of them don't even have simple things like nmap or clamav or
chkrootkit.

So... I'm going to work on porting INSERT over to USB key.  I've run in
to some strange problems.  Unfortunately, due to the nature of booting
from USB keys, I'm not sure if these problems are related to my USB key,
to my motherboard, or to the software/configuration I'm using.  :-\

From bspears at easystreet.com  Wed Dec  8 12:15:47 2004
From: bspears at easystreet.com (Bill Spears)
Date: Wed, 08 Dec 2004 12:15:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Burn some directories to a CD
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412071202410.22092-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412071202410.22092-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
Message-ID: <1102536947.19208.11.camel@apollo.spears.org>

Use the -graft-points option.

Suppose your directories are html1 and html2.

mkisofs -R -o temp.iso -graft-points dir1=html1 dir2=html2

Mount temp.iso and see if that's what you want:
mount -t iso9660 -o loop temp.iso /mnt/cdrom

On Tue, 2004-12-07 at 12:09 -0800, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> Is there a method _from the commandline_ besides installing some backup
> software to _some_ directories and their contents do a CD that will only
> be used on Linux systems.  Damn the iso9660, HPFS, Joliet, etc.
> 
> I've been looking at the man pages for over an hour but they are all
> excited about MACS and Windows systems.  I've done this before (3+ years
> ago) but lost my notes when I did a system/OS upgrade that went bad.  
> (Should have backed up better eh?  ;-)
> 
> 
> TIA,
> Rod

I really like this CD, it's designed specifically for system rescue:

http://www.sysresccd.org/index.en.php

The ISO is 110MB and it has a ton of tools, I believe it has all the 
ones you mentioned. I found it from the ntfsresize page, it has QTParted 
on it.

I have no idea if it would be possible to make it work on a USB device 
somehow instead of a CD :) But if you need a good rescue CD, I really 
recommend it.

On Monday 06 December 2004 1:40 pm, Douglas Howard wrote:

> 2. You seem to need info on partitions so you can manage them yourself.

> to find many good links on partition management.

> http://www.linux.com/howtos/Multi-Disk-HOWTO-12.shtml

On Monday 06 December 2004 3:21 pm, Rich Burroughs wrote:
> To the original poster, I know there's a section in the old RH manual
> about adding swap, it's here:
>
> http://www.redhat.com/docs/manuals/linux/RHL-8.0-Manual/custom-guide/s1-swa
>p-adding.html

Thanks, those were both helpful. Armed with what I read in them, I understood 
better what YaST's partitioning tool wanted.

First, with only an xterm window open, I turned off swapping with

swapoff -a

Then, I started up YaST. My only gripe with this tool is that, once it's main 
window is open, it doesn't give any indication that it is loading a secondary 
widow, so when I started up the partitioner, and it took 30-35 seconds before 
another window opened, I thought it wasn't working. It only took me three 
tries to figure it out, and it was only because something interrupted  me on 
the third that I found out it really was working!

There were various warnings about not changing partitions that are mounted, 
etc., but since I was working on the last three partitions, and they were not 
mounted, I forged ahead.

After deleting hda7, hda8, and hda9, I created a new hda7 at 384 Mb, 2 times 
my memory size, and set it to be my swap partition. Then, I created hda8 to 
use up the remainder. 

Two things went wrong. I'm not sure why, but the size I called for didn't 
stick, and I ended up with a swap partition that was only 192 Mb -- not much 
better than when I started. The other was that I missed seeing that the mount 
point for hda8 was /usr. So, when I finished, there were lots of commands 
that were not found, and when I tried to su to root, the locale was missing. 
Actually, I saw that it was going to use /usr, but it didn't register as a 
problem. I logged out and then back in as root on a terminal screen 
(<ctrl><alt><F1>), umounted /usr, and found the missing stuff. Then, I 
created /usr2, edited /etc/fstab to change the mount point for /dev/hda8, and 
then logged back in using X. I ran YaST again, redid the creation of hda7 and 
hda8, as above, double checking the size, and making sure hda8 used /usr2 for 
a mount point. Everything appears to have worked.

As I type, I have an xterm window open, along with patience, kmail, and k3b. 
That's the situation that gave me trouble before. Seems fine, now.

The only thing I'm not sure of is if I need to do anything with the old swap 
partition. It's sitting between the boot partition and my original main 
partition. It's not all that big, and I now have another 3.something Gb of 
additional ordinary space, so I suppose there's no problem with just leaving 
it there. It is no longer referenced in /etc/fstab, so I'm assuming it will 
just look like an unused spot in the partition table that happens to be 
formatted as linux-swap.

Thanks, again, for the pointers to the documentation.

http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

From buchholz at easystreet.com  Wed Dec  8 19:51:18 2004
From: buchholz at easystreet.com (Don Buchholz)
Date: Wed, 08 Dec 2004 19:51:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Burn some directories to a CD
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412072030300.24315-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.33.0412072030300.24315-100000@main.cyber-office.net>
Message-ID: <41B7CBB6.2060202@easystreet.com>

Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

>Rich, Carla.  Thanks for the input.  I've been there are done all that.  
>The issue was I had two directories and their files and the standard 
>mkisofs flattens them into one.  One directory has a links (with the 
>same name) to files in the other.  Mkisofs doesn't like that too much.  I 
>was also fighting the -R which kept smashing my very nice files names into 
>8.3s.  Not very useful.
>  
>
If I understand correctly, you have "/path1_to/dir1/" and "/path2_to/dir2",
and you want to create a CD with "/dir1" and "/dir2".  You need to use
"graft-points" option for mkisofs(8). 


  # mkisofs -R -J -V some_vol_name -o /path_to/cdimage.iso \
       -graft-points dir1=/path1_to/dir1  dir2=/path2_to/dir2

  # cdrecord -v -eject /path_to_cdimage.iso


- Don

From rich at paranoid.org  Wed Dec  8 22:00:18 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Wed, 08 Dec 2004 22:00:18 -0800
Subject: Resolved: [PLUG] System hangs
In-Reply-To: <200412081443.37436.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412041713.27408.rsteff.l@comcast.net>	<019301c4dbdc$46fdc220$512279a5@k61800DVH>
	<200412081443.37436.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <41B7E9F2.5050303@paranoid.org>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> The only thing I'm not sure of is if I need to do anything with the old swap 
> partition. It's sitting between the boot partition and my original main 
> partition. It's not all that big, and I now have another 3.something Gb of 
> additional ordinary space, so I suppose there's no problem with just leaving 
> it there. It is no longer referenced in /etc/fstab, so I'm assuming it will 
> just look like an unused spot in the partition table that happens to be 
> formatted as linux-swap.

Hi Richard,

That sounds like an adventure :) I'm glad it seems the problem is fixed.

You can leave the old swap partition there, it's not a problem, but you 
might as well put it in fstab. The system can use multiple swap 
partitions and swap files. I believe it will try to access the first 
entry in fstab first, that one gets the higher priority, so you could 
enter the old one second and the system would only use it if needed.

If you have access to a copy of Partition Magic you could delete it and 
expand one of the other paritions to use that space.


Rich

From rich at paranoid.org  Wed Dec  8 23:14:14 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Wed, 08 Dec 2004 23:14:14 -0800
Subject: Resolved: [PLUG] System hangs
In-Reply-To: <200412082222.22414.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412041713.27408.rsteff.l@comcast.net>	<200412081443.37436.rsteff.l@comcast.net>	<41B7E9F2.5050303@paranoid.org>
	<200412082222.22414.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <41B7FB46.1000800@paranoid.org>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Is there something special about the number? 
> In other words, if I make the priority of the old swap partition 43, is that 
> good enough?

I think I had this all screwed up :) Take a look at the man pages:

man swapon
man 2 swapon

Especially the second one. It says higher numbers mean higher priority. 
If the priority is the same they will both be used round-robin.

I don't think the order in the fstab matters.

> I think I could do that with parted, too, but I feel a little uneasy moving 
> the beginning of the next partition.

I don't think you can even move the beginning with parted if the 
filesystem is ext2 or ext3, that is one of it's limitations. You can 
only move the end point when you resize, if I'm reading the docs correctly.

> The other question I had was just what to do with the new partition? It 
> occurred to me that what I should do is to pick something, like /usr, copy 
> all of it to the new partition, rename things so the new partition is /usr, 
> and then delete the old /usr directory. The next question is which part of 
> the tree makes the most sense to give 3.something Gb of space? Maybe it 
> should be /home, since I'm expecting to have a bunch of photo images, and 
> they'd be stored in my home directory. Any recommendations along this line 
> would also be appreciated.

Well, it always makes sense to give /home it's own partition. That way 
if you decided to install a new Linux from scratch, you could skip 
formatting that partition, mount it back at /home, and all your data 
would still be there.

On multiuser boxes /var can end up being an area that grows a lot, with 
things like mail spools and logs, but it sounds like /home might be 
better for you. You can always steal some of that space later if you 
need it elsewhere.


Rich

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Thu Dec  9 00:07:43 2004
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Thu, 09 Dec 2004 00:07:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] USB Linux?
In-Reply-To: <1102469064.12274.4.camel@dell.linux.box>

On Tue, 2004-12-07 at 17:24 -0800, AthlonRob wrote:
> I just got my USB memory stick yesterday.
> 
> I want to put Linux on it.

Update:

I just got my modified INSERT running!  I finally discovered syslinux
just *wasn't* worth the hassle so I installed GRUB.  I moved things
around so GRUB could find them, booted with GRUB, discovered it couldn't
find the config file, came up with a custom "install" line, got grub
installed on the stick...

AND BAM!  IT WORKED!

So, from start to finish, I downloaded the INSERT CD and mounted it
loopback... I copied everything from it to the USB stick, modified the
init scripts inside the initrd (miniroot.gz) to load USB modules (might
not have been necessary), hardcoded the location of the cloop file
(might not have been necessary), installed grub, booted to the grub
console, reinstalled grub... and it works.

The process should be identical for virtually any of the knoppix/cloop
clones out there.  Anything cloop based should be able to use that
approximate process to become USB bootable.

I'm going to hopefully clean it up a little bit over the weekend and
write up some real documentation on it.

   Jabber: athlonrob at axpr.net   |    one can never be free.

From keith at ahapala.net  Fri Dec 10 10:15:13 2004
From: keith at ahapala.net (Keith Nasman)
Date: Fri, 10 Dec 2004 10:15:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Note keeping
Message-ID: <41B9E7B1.2000404@ahapala.net>

I've had varied success using text files to keep my notes in. I'm 
wondering if a better solution couldn't be implemented. I could write a 
LAMP solution or I could use a wiki or a notes app.

I guess my goals are:

- have categories
- keep track of each entry or change by date so that I can reference 
everything by date (i.e. what the h*ll did I do last week :-) )
- web oriented (with auth) might be nice so that I could be anywhere and 
search/update it
- I'm partial to a SQL db backend, as it would give better odds for 
dumping and importing into some other software in the future.

Maybe a private wiki would be the best way.

I'm sure many of you have already tackled this. What are you using and why?

Keith

From cstevens at gencom.us  Fri Dec 10 22:53:43 2004
From: cstevens at gencom.us (D. Cooper Stevenson)
Date: Fri, 10 Dec 2004 22:53:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Note keeping
In-Reply-To: <41B9E7B1.2000404@ahapala.net>
References: <41B9E7B1.2000404@ahapala.net>
Message-ID: <1102748023.5779.7.camel@localhost>

On Fri, 2004-12-10 at 10:15, Keith Nasman wrote:

> I've had varied success using text files to keep my notes in. I'm 
> wondering if a better solution couldn't be implemented. I could write a 
> LAMP solution or I could use a wiki or a notes app.


CRM Software may be what you're after:

  Sugar CRM
  XRMS

There are others. Once you've setup your contacts (may be imported from
SQL or comma delimited file), you may quickly add activities that are
aligned with each respective contact. 

I think one of the nicest things about CRM software is that you can
easily see the activities for a particular contact listed simply by
selecting it. This means that you can tell at a glance progress with a
particular client. 

We find CRM a great tool for making sure we stay on top of client
requests and projects.

Of course, these applications are web based have the capability for
exporting their data in standard formats.


-Coop

installed.

 

mouse  -->  KVM Switch  -->  systems

 

Win-XP  -  mouse works ok

RedHat-9  -  mouse works ok after bootup, but not after switching to another
system and then switching back.

 

strange behavior:

the pointer jumps erratically around the screen and is only fleetingly
visible, usually when it activates a screen.

redhat-config-mouse.  got the same results on all.

 

switched out the logitech for a mouse that came with an e-machines system
and problems went away.

 

If I can't get the Logitech to work, I'll have to replace it.  Not sure what
to get.

Bryan,

   Can't comment about winduhs and linux as we don't use the former at all.
However, when I first installed the LinkSys KVM and fired up the second
workstation I had very annoying ghosts and color shifts. These persisted
even with the second workstation shut down.

   A couple of sets of _very_ high quality (and equally expensive) cables [1]
fixed the problem. No ghosts, color shifts or any other eyeball-bending
displays.

HTH,

I have similar problem at work with a Dell Mouse which is made
by Logitech as well.  My KVM switch is Hawking (at work).  I want to blame 
is on my
KVM switch because I don't have the same problem
with the same mouse at home.  I am using a Linksys KVM switch.
And I am running various distros at home and work (I have redhat, fedora and 
solaris x86)

I found something weird and not sure if that helps in your case or not.
I found that the way I bend the cable to connect between KVM to my box
makes some difference.  So sometimes I would be able to fix the problem by
yanking on the cable back and forth....

On Friday 10 December 2004 2:08 pm, Bryan Murdock wrote:

> If anyone ever finds a KVM that works flawlessly switching between
> windoze and Linux, and doesn't produce ghost images on your monitors
> please let me know.

I have been successfully using a Lynksys KVM-2KIT to switch between two 
vintage 1999/2000 machines one running Linux and the other running Win98. 
Have had no problems with it.

I bought it at Pacific Solutions, on SE Powell near Foster. When I was 
shopping for it, I was looking for simple and cheap, and considered a 
mechanical switch unit. I mentioned that I had a 19" monitor, and was 
cautioned against a mechanical unit, as they did not play well with large 
monitors. Since it wasn't that much more for the all electronic Linksys unit, 
I went with that, and have been pleased. Just double click the Scroll Lock 
key and bounce back and fourth all day.

--

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Sat Dec 11 10:33:12 2004

>> 
> I bet you're right :)
> I think it falls into the realm of AT vs ATX style motherboards. I still
> hear people saying that you should leave an ATX case plugged in while
> working on it, for the sake of grounding. This only applies to old AT
> cases. Probably the same applies to our frying mouse/keyboard issue.

Straying from the topic of KVMs...

There's also the fact that since PS/2 isn't hot-pluggable, most OSes won't
notice if one is plugged in.  USB, OTOH, is hot-pluggable and (in a modern
distro at least) automatically recognized and configured.  For about
$8+S&H you can buy a USB device that allows you to connect a PS/2 keyboard
& mouse as USB: http://www.electronicsoutfitter.com/store/USB630A.html.  I
just got the Compaq branded one at Office Depot (for $20, because I didn't
want to wait and I wanted to be able to easily return it if it didn't
work) and it works fine.

Wil

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Sat Dec 11 16:04:25 2004
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 11 Dec 2004 16:04:25 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LogiTech Mouse
In-Reply-To: <41BB2C8E.90507@schluting.com>
References: <B0003188832@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
	<41BA4D4A.1070503@schluting.com>	<1102748917.12272.69.camel@dell.linux.box>
	<200412110842.46757.alex@daniloff.com> <41BB2C8E.90507@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <1102809865.12274.83.camel@dell.linux.box>

On Sat, 2004-12-11 at 09:21 -0800, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> I think it falls into the realm of AT vs ATX style motherboards. I still 
> hear people saying that you should leave an ATX case plugged in while 
> working on it, for the sake of grounding.
> This only applies to old AT cases. Probably the same applies to our 
> frying mouse/keyboard issue.

ACKKK!!  NO, NO, NO!

Static is NO LESS of a problem now than it was then.  Leaving the case
plugged in *will* help ground it, and if you keep in contact with the
case at all times, you'll be grounded... greatly reducing the chances of
some ESD damage.

Additionally, it's possible the system itself could build up a small
static charge... so if you are grounded and it is not (ie; the cable is
unplugged) *it* could discharge static to *you* - which does the same
damage.

Just be careful working around inside computers, especially when it's
dry outside.  Better safe than sorry.

-- 
Rob                                |  If not safe,
   Jabber: athlonrob at axpr.net   |    one can never be free.

cord connected will mean that the ATX motherboard is still being
powered.  Connect/disconnecting devices/cables to the motherboard
while the motherboard is powered is not recommended.

From russj at dimstar.net  Sat Dec 11 16:52:32 2004
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Sat, 11 Dec 2004 16:52:32 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LogiTech Mouse
In-Reply-To: <41BA4D4A.1070503@schluting.com>
References: <B0003188832@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
	<41BA1A7B.2070903@dimstar.net> <41BA4D4A.1070503@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <41BB9650.6080803@dimstar.net>

Charlie Schluting wrote:

> Russ Johnson wrote:
>
>> I get this with several systems I manage. I'm usually forced to 
>> "reboot" the mouse by unplugging it from the KVM.
>>
>> This is NOT recommended, and you can fry your mouse this way.
>
>
>
> Just wondering about the logic behind that.. I've never heard of 
> anyone saying "don't unplug your mouse with the computer on."
>
> I don't see how you can fry a mouse by unplugging it. Please enlighten. 

The PS/2 specification was not designed with hot plugging in mind. It's 
live, and has little to no protection for the controller chips behind it.

If you unplug the mouse, you risk the mouse, the ps/2 controller, and in 
some cases, the motherboard. If you unplug the keyboard, you risk the 
keyboard, the keyboard controller and the motherboard.

When you risk the motherboard, you risk everything that's plugged into 
it...

Unexpected use can produce unexpected results.

-- 
Russ Johnson
Dimension 7/Stargate Online
http://www.dimstar.net

Subject: [PLUG] Shutting down without a monitor

It's power outage season. Yesterday, as probably happened to one or two other 
folks, the power went out and stayed out for just over an hour. I have a 
battery backup for my two machines, but since I'm running both of them on one 
battery, I don't have my monitor plugged into it. So, when the power went 
off, the machines continued to run, but without a monitor, I couldn't use the 
mouse to initiate a shutdown. I waited a minute or so, since this had 
happened earlier, and power was restored within a few seconds, but this time 
it stayed off. When that happens, I shut down the machines. Without a 
monitor, I know what to do for my Win98 machine: <ctrl><alt><del> <tab> <tab> 
<enter> will cause Windows to go through a shut down process and power off 
the box.

Is there a similar sequence I can use for Linux?

What I did yesterday was <ctl><alt><F1>, login as root, and give the halt 
command, typing carefully so as to avoid typos. This successfully shut down 
the machine, but I had a few problems starting up again. For instance, kmail 
refused to load, saying that only one instance was permitted, and that one 
was running as pid 6xxx, according to a lock file. I deleted the lock file 
and kmail came up just fine. The other odd thing was that I had to start the 
machine twice before I could communicate over my network.

So, what I'm looking for is the proper way to initiate a shutdown that will 
cause all running programs to close in a controlled way, as if I had closed 
each one as I normally would. I've read the man page on shutdown, and it 
looks like that's what I should have used instead of halt, but I still would 
have had to switch to a terminal screen, and I'll need to make myself a note 
to remind myself of the proper command line. Does this look right?

shutdown -t now -h

The man page mentions that <ctrl><alt><del> can be used if there is an 
appropriate entry in /etc/inittab, but that one still has to be at a terminal 
screen since the "magic keys" are captured by X and "Some X11 environments 
make it possible to capture CTRL-ALT-DEL, but what exactly is done with that 
event depends on that environment."

I'm using KDE. Is there a place in that environment to set up a handler for 
<ctrl><alt><del>?

Thanks for any ideas.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Dec 13 11:17:35 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 13 Dec 2004 11:17:35 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Shutting down without a monitor
In-Reply-To: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412131111520.21453@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 13 Dec 2004, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Is there a similar sequence I can use for Linux?

Dick,

   Don't use KDE so I don't know if it responds to the ctrl-alt-backspace
chord that kills X when xfce is the desktop/window manager. Try that
some time when you can see what's happening. If it works, that's the first
thing I'd do. It should shut down all the GUI apps as X goes away.

   Next, I would 'su' to get a root prompt. You should see hard drive light
activity as a root shell is loaded. Then type either 'halt' or 'shutdown -h
now'. The first is a synonym for the latter. the syntax for 'shutdown'
requires either a '-h' (for halt) or '-r' (for reboot) and a time to
actually shut down. If your system is configured for power off when shut
down, then you'll hear the hard drive spin down and the power light go off.
Otherwise, wait for hard drive activity light to go off, press the power
switch until the system croaks and go have a beer.

Rich
