From fpikus at gmail.com  Mon Dec 13 11:23:55 2004
From: fpikus at gmail.com (Fedor Pikus)
Date: Mon, 13 Dec 2004 11:23:55 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Shutting down without a monitor
In-Reply-To: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <a4fbdc7b04121311234bace27d@mail.gmail.com>

On Mon, 13 Dec 2004 08:24:40 -0800, Richard C. Steffens
<rsteff.l at comcast.net> wrote:
> it stayed off. When that happens, I shut down the machines. Without a
> monitor, I know what to do for my Win98 machine: <ctrl><alt><del> <tab> <tab>
> <enter> will cause Windows to go through a shut down process and power off
> the box.
> 
> Is there a similar sequence I can use for Linux?
If you have recent PC and kernel, you can get ACPI support to work. By
"recent" I don't mean super-modern, I have ACPI working on a
3-years-old motherboard with 2.4.26 kernel. You may have to figure out
how to configure it after it's working, but at least on Fedora Core 1,
out of the box, pressing the power button initiates the shutdown.

Otherwise, the easiest is probably to do Ctrl-Alt-F1, Ctrl-Alt-Delete
(this initiates reboot) and then wait for the sign that PC is booting
up (usually it'll beep when it's POSTing) and power it off.

-- 
Fedor G Pikus (fpikus at gmail.com)
http://www.pikus.net
http://wild-light.com

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Dec 13 13:55:22 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 13 Dec 2004 13:55:22 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] CUPS issue
In-Reply-To: <20041213214517.51371.qmail@web50610.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041213214517.51371.qmail@web50610.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412131353510.21453@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 13 Dec 2004, Robert Kopp wrote:

>> lpr filename
>> lpr: lp: unknown printer
>
> The printer is actually called "hp." Does it need an alias or something?

Tim,

   I usually specify the printer on the command line. Use the name by which
CUPS knows it. For example, in my case I'll use,

lpr -P laser5_duplex <filename>

   and this tells CUPS to use the postscript module and duplexer in the laser
printer.

HTH,

Rich

Subject: [PLUG] Slackware Specific: Learning Why rc.local Does Not Run
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412130633260.21453@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   This is a new one on me. When the UPS battery failed and I rebooted the
system I learned that the processes, applications and drivers in rc.local do
not load. I cannot be sure that it is called at the end of the boot process,
but it has been (and is) on every other system, so I assume that it is.

   The driver for my tape drive doesn't load automatically during boot so I
put that line in rc.local, but the driver is still not loaded. gpm, httpd,
smb and a few more applications that should be loaded because /etc/rc.d/rc.*
are present and executable.

   The SCSI tape driver, sym53c8xx.o.gz, is uncommented in
/etc/rc.d/rc.modules and apparently shows up in dmesg:

sym53c895-0: rev 0x1 on pci bus 0 device 5 function 0 irq 11
sym53c895-0: Tekram format NVRAM, ID 7, Fast-40, Parity Checking
scsi1 : sym53c8xx-1.7.3c-20010512
   Vendor: TANDBERG  Model: SLR60             Rev: 0420
   Type:   Sequential-Access                  ANSI SCSI revision: 02
   Vendor: PLEXTOR   Model: CD-R   PX-W1210S  Rev: 1.01
   Type:   CD-ROM                             ANSI SCSI revision: 02
st: Version 20040102, bufsize 32768, max init. bufs 4, s/g segs 16
Attached scsi tape st0 at scsi1, channel 0, id 4, lun 0
Attached scsi CD-ROM sr0 at scsi1, channel 0, id 6, lun 0
sym53c895-0-<6,*>: FAST-20 SCSI 20.0 MB/s (50.0 ns, offset 16)
sr0: scsi3-mmc drive: 32x/32x writer cd/rw xa/form2 cdda tray
sym53c895-0-<4,*>: FAST-20 WIDE SCSI 40.0 MB/s (50.0 ns, offset 15)

But, until I manually load the driver no backups are done.

   In /etc/rc.d/rc.M (the multiuser init script) samba and gpm are called:

# Start Samba (a file/print server for Win95/NT machines).
# Samba can be started in /etc/inetd.conf instead.
if [ -x /etc/rc.d/rc.samba ]; then
   . /etc/rc.d/rc.samba start
fi

# Start the GPM mouse server:
if [ -x /etc/rc.d/rc.gpm ]; then
   . /etc/rc.d/rc.gpm start
fi

But, they don't fire up. Manual intervention is needed. Same with the
postgresql backend.

   However, many other services _do_ initialize and load properly.

   Is there a way (/var/log/messages shows me nothing meaningful) to
determinne the source of this problem and fix it? My replacement UPS battery
is scheduled to arrive today so I'd like to have the system boot properly
when I shut it down to swap UPS boxes.

>    Don't use KDE so I don't know if it responds to the ctrl-alt-backspace
> chord that kills X when xfce is the desktop/window manager. Try that
> some time when you can see what's happening. If it works, that's the first
> thing I'd do. It should shut down all the GUI apps as X goes away.

That does indeed close all running X apps, but it brings up the X login 
screen. That is good enough, though, since I can continue from there and do 
what I did before, <ctrl><alt><F1>, login as root, and type halt at the 
command prompt that, even though I can't see it, I know is there.

On Monday 13 December 2004 11:29 am, Michael Rasmussen wrote:

> You could have avoided the kmail issues by issuing an `init 3` (assuming
> RedHat SysV init style) to close X before doing your shutdown.

I didn't think of that. I did think of telinit 6, since I've used that before, 
but that would have restarted the machine. I suppose it would have done the 
same things init 3 would have done regarding X. I think the idea Rich 
suggested gets me to where I want to be.

> Does your battery backup not have a serial interface to allow power
> monitoring and automated shutdown management?

No, I got the cheap model.

On Monday 13 December 2004 11:23 am, Fedor Pikus wrote:

> If you have recent PC and kernel, you can get ACPI support to work. By
> "recent" I don't mean super-modern, I have ACPI working on a
> 3-years-old motherboard with 2.4.26 kernel. You may have to figure out
> how to configure it after it's working, but at least on Fedora Core 1,
> out of the box, pressing the power button initiates the shutdown.

My motherboard is from around 1998, and even though I'm using the 2.6 kernel, 
I don't think that idea works, unless there's a setting I could change. I 
accidentally hit the power button when reaching for the reset button a week 
or two ago, and the machine powered down.

> Otherwise, the easiest is probably to do Ctrl-Alt-F1, Ctrl-Alt-Delete
> (this initiates reboot) and then wait for the sign that PC is booting
> up (usually it'll beep when it's POSTing) and power it off.

Yes, that should work; I do hear the POST beeps on reboot. While it might be 
slightly quicker, the process outlined above gets me to a powered down state, 
and the note stuck to the side of the monitor is pretty short.

Thanks for all your ideas. Now, I'm ready for the next power outage.

end of the file. Those are similar to keystroke commands in emacs, joe and
LyX. When I check in the Tool->Configuration dialog box I see function names
associated with menu items, but no key short-cuts.

   There is no place in 'qtconfig' where the keystroke behavior could be
changed.

   Has anyone else experienced this or has a thought on what broke?

TIA,

Rich

Portable Linux Enthusiast,

EmperorLinux Inc. announces our newest ultra-mobile Linux workstation.
The Kiwi T1x0, based on the Sony VAIO T140, T150 T160, or T170, comes
pre-configured with Linux:  http://www.EmperorLinux.com/mfgr/sony/kiwi
The Kiwi T1 is the first 3-pound Linux machine to break the XGA barrier
with an amazingly bright 1280x768 wide-aspect LCD (10.6").  X runs this
LCD in the full native mode.  It is packed with additional features,
including Firewire, WiFi a/b/g, and DVD-RW burner. Configurations start
under $2400.

This new Kiwi follows in the footsteps of the original Kiwi systems
based on the Sony Picturebook C1VP and C1MW.  It is still just larger
that a VHS tape, smaller than most O'Reilly books (10.7"x8.1"x1.1"), and
highly Linux compatible.

EmperorLinux has certified the Kiwi T150 for Fedora, Red Hat Enterprise,
Debian, Slackware, and SuSE.  Full hardware support under all these
Linux distributions is made possible by the "empkernel", the custom
built Linux kernel for laptops from EmperorLinux.  Of course the system
can also be configured dual-boot Linux/Windows with a shared data
partition if required.

The Kiwi T150 comes well equipped for the portable power user:
         - 1.1 GHz Pentium-M 733 CPU w/2MB cache
         - 512 - 1024 MB RAM
         - 40 GB hard drive
         - 1280x768, 10.6" wide-aspect, X-Bright LCD
         - CDRW-DVD or DVD-RW drive

This wide range of hardware specifications makes the Kiwi a perfect
match for anyone wanting a super-slim, ultra-lite Linux workstation.
Customers in academia and the corporate sector, as well as students and
hobbyists can find a Kiwi to fit their needs.

The Kiwi T150 has full Linux support for:
         - X @ 1280x768, 24bpp, i855gm (w/"xrandr" and external VGA)
         - Internal 10/100 land-line ethernet (RJ-45) (eepro100)
         - Internal 802.11 a/b/g WiFi ethernet 11-54Mbps (ipw2200)
         - USB 2.0 (memory keys, mice, floppies, keyboards, ...)
         - IEEE1394 FireWire (external hard drives, DV cameras,...)
         - CardBus cards (WiFi, modem, SCSI, ...)
         - ACPI Hibernate (via swsusp)

Complete Kiwi specifications, accessories, pricing, and availability
are listed on our web-site: http://www.EmperorLinux.com/mfgr/sony/kiwi

Feel free to call or email if you need a full quote, have any technical
questions, or are ready to place an order.  We can talk about the
hardware support and capabilities of the system, and various accessories
too.  Our technical staff will happily discuss with you what _you_ need
from a Linux ultra-portable.

This looks very handy, but its dependencies are a nightmare.  I fixed
the spec file by including the following dependencies:

BuildRequires:  gtk-sharp-devel
BuildRequires:  mono
Requires:     mono

However, I seem to have hit an impasse.  The configure script calls
"pkg-config --cflags gnome-sharp", which fails since there is no
/usr/lib/pkgconfig/gnome-sharp.pc file.  The /usr/lib/gnome-sharp.dll
file exists as part of the gtk-sharp package, but the .pc file is not
present in the gtk-sharp-devel package.

My best friend for such things, Google, has not come through for me:

http://www.google.com/search?q=gnome-sharp.pc

For those of you who have Tomboy working, where does the
/usr/lib/pkgconfig/gnome-sharp.pc file come from?

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec 15 13:08:42 2004
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 15 Dec 2004 13:08:42 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Projector prices
In-Reply-To: <20041215200010.0656B5A6F@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20041215200010.0656B5A6F@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <20041215210842.GA17291@gate.kl-ic.com>


What follows is slightly off topic, but:

Rich writes:
>    This brings up a question that's been on my mind for a while. Why hasn't
> the price of LCD projectors dropped tremendously? Computers, hard drives,
> RAM, digital cameras, printers and all sorts of computing hardware have
> decreased by orders of magnitude over the past few years. Why not the LCD
> projectors?


Ron Braithwaite <ron at nutriware.com> writes:
> They have dropped - except for the price of the bulb, which often is
> $400 or more. Until a new lighting source is perfected (perhaps
> lithographed displays on cloth?), the price isn't going to drop much
> more.


Rich writes again:
>    Oh, my. I'm surprised no one's seen this as a business opportunity. I've
> no time -- or inclination -- to pursue why the bulbs are so expensive, but
> it seems odd to me because these projectors are used in "home theaters" as
> well as businesses so the market for an inexpensive unit should be very
> large.

Remember that old Proxima 2810 that Zot had, that we tried to use at A.T.
a couple of years ago?  That 640x480 piece of [fertilizer] cost $7,495
new in 1997.  The Infocus LP70 I will be bringing is 1024x768 for
$1,895 new (I bought it for less than half of that, used).   The
new unit is smaller, much easier to use and more versatile, and
makes a much better picture, 4 times as bright.  

The downside IS the bulbs - the 300 lumen bulbs for the old projector
are close to standard overhead projector bulbs, and cost $30 or so,
lasting about 500 hours.  The 1200 lumen bulbs for the new projector
are $500, very specialized, and are claimed to last 3000 hours.  The
cost per lumen-hour is about the same.  There are outfits that will
rebuild these lamp assemblies for $200 or so.

Why the high price?  Part of it is that you are buying a whole specialized
assembly, with lots of extra pieces and a lower volume.  The justification
is probably that handling the inner bulb directly is likely to damage it.
The inner bulb is a real high tech affair, containing an extremely small
and extremely bright arc lamp, requiring exotic chemistry and a special
and highly tuned ballast to get the long lifetime.  There is a LOT more
science in there than in the old overhead projector bulbs.  

That said, T.I., the makers of the DLP micro-mirror display that forms
the core of my unit, realizes that the high cost of lamps means their
is less money for DLPs.  So they've put together a working group of
manufacturers to define some standard bulbs and cages, hoping to reduce
the costs greatly:

http://www.dlp.com/images/downloads/RPTV_Lamp_Working_Group_21April2004.pdf

When the lamp is standardized, the volumes are high, and multiple
manufacturers make them, you can expect costs of lamps to plummet.  
That, and other improvements in the projectors, may drop the price
of a unit similar to my own down to $1K in a year or so.  My inside
sources say that InFocus is really struggling to make a profit on
these little guys.  The innards come from a dozen different countries
on four continents.

At the end of the day, though, the reason why these units cost so much
is that the typical customer will pay that much for better and better
units.  The higher lumen, higher resolution units still cost $50K for
7700 lumens at 1600x1200.  Such units are used in concert halls and
arenas.  The biggest displays are made by AdVan over in Vancouver,
which is a wall of arrayed projectors (12 or so?) mounted in a truck. 
Bright, hyper-high resolution displays that work in the Big Blue Room.
Perhaps we can get Steve Hix to loan us one for an Advanced Picnic
next summer. :-)

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From ian at znark.com  Wed Dec 15 22:05:33 2004
From: ian at znark.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Wed, 15 Dec 2004 22:05:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Shutting down without a monitor
In-Reply-To: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <41C125AD.2030304@znark.com>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> It's power outage season. Yesterday, as probably happened to one or two other 
> folks, the power went out and stayed out for just over an hour. I have a 
> battery backup for my two machines, but since I'm running both of them on one 
> battery, I don't have my monitor plugged into it. So, when the power went 
> off, the machines continued to run, but without a monitor, I couldn't use the 
> mouse to initiate a shutdown. I waited a minute or so, since this had 
> happened earlier, and power was restored within a few seconds, but this time 
> it stayed off. When that happens, I shut down the machines. Without a 
> monitor, I know what to do for my Win98 machine: <ctrl><alt><del> <tab> <tab> 
> <enter> will cause Windows to go through a shut down process and power off 
> the box.
> 
> Is there a similar sequence I can use for Linux?
> 

What I do on my headless Debian box is setup the keys Alt-UpArrow to 
halt the machine.  This is similar to the Ctrl-Atl-Del keypress.  You 
will need to be at the Linux console.  This is done by adding to 
/etc/inittab:

# Action on special keypress (ALT-UpArrow).
kb::kbrequest:/sbin/shutdown -t1 -a -h now

  - Ian

-- 
ian at znark.com
http://www.znark.com/

Yes.  For Carla's book, the O'R UG discount is 20%, while the Walmart
and Amazon discount is 40%, and the shipping is cheaper.  The discount
differs depending on the item, of course.  Addall also checks for
remaindered and used copies;  I have bought recent books for 5% of new,
the cost dominated by normal shipping charges.  

The addall project seems to be very much the same spirit as FOSS, except
applied to shopping for books and other media.  I think they even use a
Linux server.

Virus Alert: Zafi.d prevention and cure 

Name: Zafi.d also known as w32.zafi.d at mm and Erkez on Symantec
Rank: 6 (out of 10) on CNET virus meter
Type: e-mail worm
Impact of vulnerability: Opens port 8181 for remote access
How it works: E-mail contains an exe or zip file attachment
How to recognize: Message contains a Christmas greeting with an attachment
Virus brief <http://ct.com.com/click?q=52-LU0oIrJ4Is3EBMsELgv1tOCkrEmb>  
Zafi.d (w32.zafi.d at mm, also known as Erkez on Symantec) is 11,745 bytes in
size, with about 30KB of assembly code. This "seasonal" worm can also spread
by shared network folders. Zafi.d attempts to shut down antivirus and
firewall defenses on an infected computer and will open a port for remote
computer access. Zafi.d does not affect Mac OS, Linux, or UNIX systems. For
details see Zafi.d
<http://ct.com.com/click?q=b3-QH8xQYKl7eMPCn4aPacbD~fNED45>  prevention and
cure. 

More on Zafi.d

purports to be Christmas greeting 
*	Virus <http://ct.com.com/click?q=89-h2zbQ~J3rVjLV5yxpwDQBaM4LQYl>

-------- Original Message --------
Subject: Fedora Project launches Pre-Extras
Date: Thu, 16 Dec 2004 14:00:26 -0500
From: seth vidal <skvidal at fedoraproject.org>
Reply-To: For users of Fedora Core releases <fedora-list at redhat.com>
To: fedora-devel-list at redhat.com, fedora-announce-list at redhat.com, 
fedora-test-list at redhat.com, fedora-list at redhat.com
CC: lwn at lwn.net

The Fedora Project is officially launching Fedora Pre-Extras, to work
alongside Fedora Core 3 for i386 and x86_64 based platforms.

What is Pre-Extras?
It is our way of showing you that we've been working hard, as many
packages have been built, and are ready for distribution.

We are working very hard to make the build system work well, and the
infrastructure for this is almost ready, it just has a few kinks that we
are ironing out. As a measure of good faith, CVS for both Core and
Extras has already been opened.

While waiting for Extras to officially launch, use Pre-Extras today!

Where did all these packages come from?
These were packages from the now-merged Fedora.us repository as well as
freshrpms.net repository. So they maintain the same high quality
standards that was imposed previously.

So how do I get it?
Pre-Extras is currently hosted on the FedoraProject.org website, and is
available at:

	http://fedoraproject.org/pre-extras/3/

It is available as a YUM repository, and an entry like the following
would suffice (in /etc/yum.conf):

[pre-extras]
name=Pre Extras
baseurl=http://fedoraproject.org/pre-extras/3/$basearch/
gpgcheck=1

There is a temporary Pre Extras GPG key, which all packages are signed
with. The fingerprint:

pub  1024D/1AC70CE6 2004-12-14 Fedora Pre Extras Release 
<pre-extras at fedoraproject.org>
      Key fingerprint = 5389 DD00 C5BC 5168 12B4  3272 82ED 9504 1AC7 0CE6
sub  1024g/4E1A9D43 2004-12-14

To import the key:
	
  rpm --import 
http://fedoraproject.org/pre-extras/RPM-GPG-KEY-Fedora-Pre-Extras

What about bugs?
If you find bugs in the packages, report them at the Fedora Extras
Bugzilla:

	http://bugzilla.redhat.com/beta/

A cautionary note...
To paraphrase a Fedora Project contributor, Jef Spaleta, these packages
could eat babies; that is to say, they are built, signed, and ready for
use, but they have not gone through the rigorous QA testing that would
be expected normally from Extras. So if they break, submit a bug report,
but there are no implied warranties or guarantees that they will work.
Use these packages at your own risk!





From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Dec 16 13:54:39 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 16 Dec 2004 13:54:39 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Projector prices
In-Reply-To: <20041216142241.4A7DA5A6B@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200412162154.iBGLsdPx048280@m5p.com>

>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> Remember that old Proxima 2810 that Zot had, that we tried to use at A.T.
> a couple of years ago?  That 640x480 piece of [fertilizer] cost $7,495
> new in 1997.  The Infocus LP70 I will be bringing is 1024x768 for
> $1,895 new (I bought it for less than half of that, used).   The
> new unit is smaller, much easier to use and more versatile, and
> makes a much better picture, 4 times as bright.  

About 2 years ago another group decided it was time to get a projector
and went for a $3K budget. They got a projector that was quite a bit
larger than Keith's (imagine a 4 inch thick laptop), but still did
1024x768, produced 2000 lumens and had a lot of image adjustments
(notably zoom and keystone). Figure that with a 10 foot image it is
visible even with florescent lights directly above the screen (washed
out, but visible).

Appears 1000 lumens is now the standard, and the brighter projectors are
fairly common at this point. Other nice bit is that DLPs have a *much*
better response time than LCDs.

> At the end of the day, though, the reason why these units cost so much
> is that the typical customer will pay that much for better and better
> units.  The higher lumen, higher resolution units still cost $50K for
> 7700 lumens at 1600x1200.  Such units are used in concert halls and
> arenas.  The biggest displays are made by AdVan over in Vancouver,
> which is a wall of arrayed projectors (12 or so?) mounted in a truck. 
> Bright, hyper-high resolution displays that work in the Big Blue Room.
> Perhaps we can get Steve Hix to loan us one for an Advanced Picnic
> next summer. :-)

I believe some 4000 lumen projectors can be had for less than $10K.
Another reason the high end projectors cost so much is they have 3 DLPs
rather than the single DLP of the low end projectors. DLP panels are
*very* expensive, yet because they can respond so fast, it is reasonable
to use a color wheel and flicker a single panel in synch with the wheel,
rather than having 3 panels (wrap your head around that one!).


>From: Jeme A Brelin <jeme at brelin.net>
> On Wed, 15 Dec 2004, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> > When the lamp is standardized, the volumes are high, and multiple
> > manufacturers make them, you can expect costs of lamps to plummet.
> > That, and other improvements in the projectors, may drop the price of a
> > unit similar to my own down to $1K in a year or so.
> 
> Now, it's been my belief for some time that all this talk about HDTV and
> those fancy new plasma screens is moot because the REAL trend is going to
> be toward a multimedia PC (that will eventually foil any DRM attempt) and
> a decent projector (being more portable and having, to some extent,
> variable screen size).

Plasma the real problem is severe burn in, the two advantages are they're
relatively cheap for large sizes and look great. Thing is projection LCDs
and DLPs don't don't burn in and they can do quite large. At this point
it looks like gas plasma is dying out, though it is going to take several
years before you can't buy them anymore.

> Does anybody know the power consumption of one of these projectors?  I
> imagine it takes a whole lot of juice to throw 1200 lumens and that
> doesn't even include the electronics and such.  How does it compare with a
> big plasma screen?  To a CRT?

I think 1000 lumen projectors run less than 350 watts. I think the 2000
lumen projector was 550 watts or so. I don't have numbers for the others,
but I believe that is similar to plasma screens of comparable size. No
idea about CRTs (then again do you really want to battle with burn in and
tremendous weight of a rear-projection CRT?).

From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Dec 16 15:02:14 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 16 Dec 2004 15:02:14 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Projector prices
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412161420370.25191@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <200412162302.iBGN2EK7049300@m5p.com>

> > Appears 1000 lumens is now the standard, and the brighter projectors are
> > fairly common at this point. Other nice bit is that DLPs have a *much*
> > better response time than LCDs.

>    For the non-hardware enabled, like me, please explain "DLP".

Google should provide the answer, Texas Instruments's "Digital Light
Processor". The first commercial MEMS (Micro-Electro-Mechanical System)
device on the market. It is a piece of silicon with an array of tiny
mirrors on it. The major trick is building something which moves the
mirrors around on a piece of silicon. There have been plenty of
laboratory experiments moving things around merely with static charges,
but few of those ever reached commercial production. The only other
commercial MEMS device I've seen mention of is sensors on some airbags.

DLPs are quite expensive (I heard nearly $1K for a single 1280x1024
panel), but move very fast (at a previous PLUG meeting I asked Keith
Packard whether it was possible to make one operate at 600Hz, and his
answer was yes). The one downfall is they are strictly small reflective
devices. You cannot make a direct-view DLP, nor can you make one that you
shine light through.

From rsteff.l at comcast.net  Thu Dec 16 21:56:24 2004

Subject: [PLUG] Shutting down without a monitor
In-Reply-To: <41C125AD.2030304@znark.com>
References: <200412130824.40677.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
	<41C125AD.2030304@znark.com>
Message-ID: <200412162156.25209.rsteff.l@comcast.net>

On Wednesday 15 December 2004 10:05 pm, Ian Burrell wrote:

> What I do on my headless Debian box is setup the keys Alt-UpArrow to
> halt the machine.  This is similar to the Ctrl-Atl-Del keypress.  You
> will need to be at the Linux console.  This is done by adding to
> /etc/inittab:
>
> # Action on special keypress (ALT-UpArrow).
> kb::kbrequest:/sbin/shutdown -t1 -a -h now

I was going to ask if this is something unique to Debian, or if it is 
available across various distributions. Then, I realized that I should look 
at /sbin, to confirm that I have shutdown, and also look in /etc/inittab to 
see if there is some hint in there about handling special keyboard 
combinations. Here's what I have:

# special keyboard request (Alt-UpArrow)
# look into the kbd-0.90 docs for this
kb::kbrequest:/bin/echo "Keyboard Request -- edit /etc/inittab to let this 
work."

So, I could just replace the sample I have with what you use, and have the 
same capability, except that I'd still have to be at a terminal screen, where 
it does indeed work, even if not logged in, so I'd still have to log in. But, 
I wouldn't have had to shut down X first, so it reduces the process by a 
step.

Thanks for sharing this technique.

--

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Fri Dec 17 09:08:10 2004
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Fri, 17 Dec 2004 09:08:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] how to tell when the filesystem is borken?
In-Reply-To: <200412162342.38606.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200412162342.38606.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <31450-89520@sneakemail.com>

On Thu, 16 Dec 2004 23:42:38 -0800, Carla Schroder wrote:

> So how can you tell when it's the filesystem corrupting your data, and not
> something else?

Here's how I tell:

1) Identify that corruption is taking place

$ dd if=/dev/random of=/path/to/file/on/broken/filesystem bs=1024k count=10

Adjust count as needed.  Generally I've found that if things are bad
enough even a new small file will be corrupted.

$ md5sum (or cksum) /path/to/file/on/broken/filesystem
<some value>
$ md5sum (or cksum) /path/to/file/on/broken/filesystem
<a different value>

2) Isolate it to the filesystem

$ umount /path
$ md5sum (or cksum) /dev/<device for broken filesystem>
<some value>
$ md5sum (or cksum) /dev/<device for broken filesystem>
<same value>

If the checksums on an unmounted device differ, then you have a
problem with your hardware.  (I've only seen this happen with bad RAID
controllers, usually JBOD devices either work or shoot out I/O
errors.)

Other things that indicate filesystem-level troubles can be found via
"fsck", but most filesystems recover well from these minor glitches. 
Lots of these problems can indicate filesystem corruption.

  -- Steve

>   I'm on a Slackware system. SpamAssassin-2.64 was installed from CPAN. I

> cpan

> download
> a tarball and do it manually?
>
>   I ask because I don't want to break what's sort-of working here and get
> distracted having to fix what I broke. 

I just did this. (Today...)

I followed the instructions in the upgrade file, which got 3.0.2 installed.

Then I watched my incoming mail for a short time. Long enough to 
determine that spamc/spamd weren't doing anything... Looking at top, I 
see 5 spamd processes just spinning.

So, I looked for some links.

I found this: http://www.akadia.com/services/postfix_spamassassin.html

I also had a rule file (/etc/mail/spamassassin/10_local_report.cf) with 
a bunch of rules that didn't pass when I ran 'spamassassin --lint'.

So I ended up going through the website above to reconfigure 
spamassassin and postfix to work with the new SA.

Upgrading my bayes database ended up being as easy as sa-learn --sync, 
but I'm not sure it's using that database... SA is now running as it's 
own user, so I think I'm using a global bayes database.

Not entirely painless, but it appears to be working now. It took about 
two hours to do all of it.

Russ

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Dec 17 13:37:16 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 17 Dec 2004 13:37:16 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] how to tell when the filesystem is borken?
In-Reply-To: <20041217185325.269765A7A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200412172137.iBHLbGpU068755@m5p.com>

>From: Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com>
> A guaranteed way to generate a heated discussion is to say "But I like 
> ReiserFS." 'Cause then you'll get all kinds of folks claiming that Reiser 
> broke and ate their data. Which may be true, I dunno, because none of these 
> have been able to explain how they knew it was a defective filesystem, and 
> not a dying hard drive, or power problems, or flakey memory, or a worm or 
> virus, or a weirdo application.... you get the idea.

Not precisely the answer you were looking for, but there are three things
I dislike about ReiserFS in its current state:

1> There is no dump program. Saying that GNU tar works, while mostly
true, is not an acceptable answer. True dump programs tend to work much
better than tar can. This may be less of a concern to some folks, it is
not acceptable for my main systems. This is a fatal weakness for me.

2> They cannot mount read-only. I've been led to believe ext3 behaves
similarly, but if I tell the filesystem to mount read-only, failing to
mount is acceptable but silently replaying the journal is not. Given how
the filesystem works (they cannot mount an unclean filesystem), this is
an understandable decision, but I'd far rather they obey read-only and
provide a mode for journal playback-only. I find this disgusting, but not
a fatal flaw.

3> As Russell Senior mentioned, filesystems should deal with some
hardware funkyness. I tend to make very sure I've got working hardware,
but failures do occur. ReiserFS is a bit fragile (but *very* fast, you
determine the tradeoff).


> So how can you tell when it's the filesystem corrupting your data, and not 
> something else?

Steve Bonds gave some good starting points. Hardware can be very sneaky
on occasion though. The one I've seen was the Conner 1060 (I think it was
Conner, might of been another manufacturer) drives. They had a firmware
bug that ext2 was somehow able to tickle (they fixed it, but you had to
figure out that it wasn't ext2's fault). Those drives would of easily
passed Steve's tests, swapping to them worked fine, MinixFS worked fine,
UFS worked fine, but somehow ext2 caused the drives to go wacky.

Funky error messages with no apparent cause can be a sign of hardware
problems.


--

Josh Orchard wrote:
> Hello all,
> 
> Does anyone know of a limit in either apache or DNS(bind 9) for a
> large amount of sites?  I'm looking at 250+ websites and wanted to
> know if there is a problem or limit to hosting that many sites on one
> server.

Hi Josh,

There's not a limit on a number of sites, no. You can host 250+ web 
sites all on the same box using Apache and Bind, I've done it before. 
The kind of performance you'll get will depend on the hardware you use, 
and also the needs of the sites themselves (how much traffic, are they 
running a lot of code, etc.)


Rich

From lowbassman at gmail.com  Fri Dec 17 16:16:59 2004
From: lowbassman at gmail.com (Matt Alexander)
Date: Fri, 17 Dec 2004 17:16:59 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Authenticating Linux systems to MS AD using Kerberos
Message-ID: <9e0a35780412171616256ee500@mail.gmail.com>

If you have a Microsoft AD controller and you'd like to authenticate
your Linux systems to it, here's a simple way:

These instructions are taken from a RedHat box so some things might be
different for other distros:

Edit /etc/krb5.conf and change all the domain/realm info to your
company's values.  Make sure these are in capital letters because the
connection will fail for some stupid Microsoft reason if not.  The
"kdc" line is your AD controller.

Next, edit /etc/pam.d/system-auth to include this line after the auth
line with pam_env.so:
auth sufficient /lib/security/pam_krb5.so

And change the auth line for pam_unix.so to this:
auth sufficient /lib/security/pam_unix.so likeauth nullok use_first_pass

You need to make sure the Linux box and the AD controller have
approximately the same time or else the Kerberos authentication will
fail.  Use ntp or rdate or whatever to keep them in sync.

Now all that you need on the Linux box is the account name.  The
password you supply will be the password stored in AD.
~M

-- 
Get Firefox!
http://getfirefox.com/

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Sat Dec 18 08:48:49 2004
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 18 Dec 2004 08:48:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] DNS or Apache limits

	<41C353B6.9090708@paranoid.org> <20041217223641.GC1600@rauhaus.org>

Message-ID: <1103388530.13047.22.camel@dell.linux.box>

On Fri, 2004-12-17 at 23:25 -0800, Stafford A. Rau wrote:
> * AthlonRob <AthlonRob at axpr.net> [041217 20:34]:
> > See:  https://mail.axpr.net
> > and:  https://imp.axpr.net
> 
> Those sites return:
> 
> "Unable to verify the identity of localhost as a trusted site. Please
> notify the site's webmaster about this problem."
> 
> So you can do it, but only in a broken fashion.

Yes.  As I understand it, an HTTPS connection is encrypted from the
getgo, before any HTTP information is sent, so Apache can't know what
host you're looking for until after the SSL key is sent to the client,
which identifies the server.  The server identifies itself before the
client identifies what server it is looking for.

It *IS*, however, still SSL encrypted.  The certificate is no good, but
you otherwise enjoy all the other benefits of an SSL encrypted
connection.

> >From http://httpd.apache.org/docs-2.0/vhosts/name-based.html:
> 
> "Name-based virtual hosting cannot be used with SSL secure servers
> because of the nature of the SSL protocol."

Which is not 100% accurate.  You can't use name-based virtual hosting
with SSL without each site sharing the same SSL key.  It works, but is
not ideal.

From griffint at pobox.com  Sat Dec 18 19:40:19 2004
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Sat, 18 Dec 2004 19:40:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] smbfs slowness
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.52.0412181657100.12353@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.52.0412181657100.12353@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <200412181940.19684.griffint@pobox.com>

On Saturday 18 December 2004 4:58 pm, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> OK, so, I just used smbmount to connect to a WinXP machine that someone
> put in my basement (long story).
>
> Anyway, it's excruciatingly slow.  I mean, bytes per second.
>
> What gives?  I don't know enough about smb to even know where to check.
>
> J.

This almost certainly won't help but it's a easy thing to check. Try
mounting with a CIFS file system instead of an SMBFS file system. CIFS
is Microsoft's replacement for the SMB protocol. You can use
'mount -t cifs' or 'mount.cifs'. See mount.cifs(8) for details.

The other thing you can do is try some other protocols for transferring
data and see how they do. For example from the Windows box you could
connect back to the Linux box with FTP or HTTP and transfer some large
files. That will tell you if it's a general networking problem or
something specific to the Windows file share.

How are your ping statistics?

Terry



From rich at paranoid.org  Sat Dec 18 21:15:21 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Sat, 18 Dec 2004 21:15:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] DNS or Apache limits
In-Reply-To: <20041219032238.GB1244@rauhaus.org>
References: <3132.216.99.218.67.1103319125.squirrel@216.99.218.67>	<41C353B6.9090708@paranoid.org>
	<20041217223641.GC1600@rauhaus.org>	<1103344432.13052.15.camel@dell.linux.box>	<20041218072527.GA15867@rauhaus.org>	<1103388530.13047.22.camel@dell.linux.box>
	<20041219032238.GB1244@rauhaus.org>
Message-ID: <41C50E69.4020400@paranoid.org>

Stafford A. Rau wrote:
> * AthlonRob <AthlonRob at axpr.net> [041218 08:49]:
> 
>>It *IS*, however, still SSL encrypted.  The certificate is no good, but
>>you otherwise enjoy all the other benefits of an SSL encrypted
>>connection.
> 
> Ok, that makes sense.

Also, not every site is going to want/need SSL. In my experience working 
for web hosting companies, most do not. So, the limit Stafford mentioned 
could be stretched to host many more than 255 sites, since most would 
not need their own IP.

As for the issue with certs, I think it depends a lot on what you're 
doing. If the SSL is for someone doing e-commerce, then I think you want 
to have everything working without any messages that might scare someone 
away. If you're talking about having SSL to protect the logins for the 
users to access their admin area, webmail, etc., then maybe you can get 
away with what Rob describes. You might even be able to coach users to 
install the key for the CA in their browser so there's no error.

Most of the situations I've been in used name based virtual hosting for 
the mass of customers, and then had a specific box with SSL capability 
for those who needed it.

I don't think I've ever hosted more than 255 sites on a Linux box, 
actually, but I'm sure it's doable. I've seen FreeBSD boxes handle 
400-500 sites with suitable hardware.


Rich




From heinlein at madboa.com  Sat Dec 18 21:33:14 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sat, 18 Dec 2004 21:33:14 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] DNS or Apache limits
In-Reply-To: <41C50E69.4020400@paranoid.org>
References: <3132.216.99.218.67.1103319125.squirrel@216.99.218.67>
	<41C353B6.9090708@paranoid.org> <20041217223641.GC1600@rauhaus.org>
	<1103344432.13052.15.camel@dell.linux.box>
	<20041218072527.GA15867@rauhaus.org>
	<1103388530.13047.22.camel@dell.linux.box>
	<20041219032238.GB1244@rauhaus.org> <41C50E69.4020400@paranoid.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412182122240.2498@pecos.madboa.com>

On Sat, 18 Dec 2004, Rich Burroughs wrote:

> Also, not every site is going to want/need SSL.

If all you need to do is pass otherwise useless authentication info 
like web-only usernames and passwords, it's worth investigating digest 
authentication.

Basic authentication, the standard web-auth technique, is insecure; 
it's no more than base64-encoded plaintext.

Digest authentication is considerably more secure; it's based on an 
MD5-encoded response to a one-time nonce.

Apache and most modern browsers support digest authentication; it's 
definitely worth a look-see.

<frustrated-grumble>

   My headless SPARC doesn't a good entropy pool; there are no
   pseudo-random X events, and the network and disk drivers I use don't
   collect entropy either.

   All of which means that Apache hangs after a reboot if I've enabled
   mod_digest.

   Sparc/Linux needs a better set of entropy-gathering drivers.

</frustrated-grumble>

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

On Monday 20 December 2004 8:37 am, Bill Thoen wrote:

> I was trying to locate a thread posted here last summer/sping about using
> Procmail, but when I went to http://lists.pdxlinux.org/pipermail/plug/ it
> seems that there is no convenient search facility for the archives. It
> seems a bit much to have to downlaod the entire raw archives just to
> search for one thread, but is there a better way?

Google is your friend. Go to the Google advanced search page, put in whatever 
you want search for, Procmail, for example, and then go down near the bottom 
of the page and enter:

pdxlinux.org

in the box for restricting the search to a specific domain.

You could also just put this into a Google search box:

Procmail site:pdxlinux.org

and get the same result.

There are 383 entries for Procmail on the site.

Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Dec 20 11:27:34 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 20 Dec 2004 11:27:34 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Searching the Archives

Message-ID: <pan.2004.12.20.19.27.34.81267@nakedape.cc>

On Mon, 20 Dec 2004 09:37:47 -0700, Bill Thoen wrote:

> I was trying to locate a thread posted here last summer/sping about using
> Procmail, but when I went to http://lists.pdxlinux.org/pipermail/plug/ it
> seems that there is no convenient search facility for the archives. It
> seems a bit much to have to downlaod the entire raw archives just to
> search for one thread, but is there a better way?

We should get this on the web site, but I've added a feed to Gmane:
http://dir.gmane.org/gmane.org.user-groups.linux.portland

If you've never used Gmane, it's a mail-to-NNTP gateway and web archive. 
NNTP is the protocol used for reading Usenet news, so any news reader
like the one built into Mozilla/Thunderbird or Pan or Knode works with it.
 Gmane also has a couple of really nice web interfaces to the archives,
 RSS feeds and searching.

You might notice that I'm actually reading the list through it :)

Wil

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Dec 20 13:59:15 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 20 Dec 2004 13:59:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Authenticating Linux systems to MS AD using Kerberos
References: <9e0a35780412171616256ee500@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <pan.2004.12.20.21.59.14.229364@nakedape.cc>

On Fri, 17 Dec 2004 17:16:59 -0700, Matt Alexander wrote:

> If you have a Microsoft AD controller and you'd like to authenticate your
> Linux systems to it, here's a simple way:
> 
> These instructions are taken from a RedHat box so some things might be
> different for other distros:
> 
> Edit /etc/krb5.conf and change all the domain/realm info to your company's
> values.  Make sure these are in capital letters because the connection
> will fail for some stupid Microsoft reason if not.  The "kdc" line is your
> AD controller.
> 
> Next, edit /etc/pam.d/system-auth to include this line after the auth line
> with pam_env.so:
> auth sufficient /lib/security/pam_krb5.so
> 
> And change the auth line for pam_unix.so to this: auth sufficient
> /lib/security/pam_unix.so likeauth nullok use_first_pass
> 
> You need to make sure the Linux box and the AD controller have
> approximately the same time or else the Kerberos authentication will fail.
>  Use ntp or rdate or whatever to keep them in sync.
> 
> Now all that you need on the Linux box is the account name.  The password
> you supply will be the password stored in AD. ~M

Frightfully enough, Microsoft has published a doc on doing it:

http://www.microsoft.com/windows2000/techinfo/planning/security/kerbsteps.asp

And on a RH box, you should/could just run 'authconfig' to do that setup. 
Actually, though, the pam.d/system-auth file works better if you tweak it
a bit:  You really want pam_krb5 before pam_unix (and/or pam_ldap) and to
move 'use_first_pass' to the latter modules.

Also, you can use the 'net' command to create the host key in Active
Directory and copy it to the local keytab; otherwise you end up using a
less defined process to get the host's key from a w2k workstation into the
key tab.

There's also fun stuff you can do with winbind to automatically map W2K
SIDs to UID/GIDs and with OpenLDAP (or apparently now MySQL) site-wide.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley                                 wcooley at nakedape.cc
Naked Ape Consulting                        http://nakedape.cc
* * * * Linux, UNIX, Networking and Security Solutions * * * *

"Sufficiently advanced cluelessness is indistinguishable from malice." 
 -- Alex Martelli

interested in sftp or scp solutions if possible.

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Dec 20 15:05:31 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 20 Dec 2004 15:05:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: how to tell when the filesystem is borken?
References: <200412162342.38606.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <pan.2004.12.20.23.05.26.997461@nakedape.cc>

On Thu, 16 Dec 2004 23:42:38 -0800, Carla Schroder wrote:

> So how can you tell when it's the filesystem corrupting your data, and not
> something else?

Short of hardware failure, the filesystem is responsible if you're getting
directories that aren't functioning properly or files that seem to be
files but aren't.  Or you've got data files from multiple applications
that are corrupted.  Using the SMART features of your drive should help
you determine if the drive itself is having problems.  I guess there was a
time when a couple of the IDE chipsets could mess things up, but I don't
think any of my systems ever had them.  If you've got write-caching
enabled on your drive (some IDE drives default to having it on) and you
have power source problems then you could also get corruption caused by
hardware.

How would you know which it is?  Intuition I guess.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley                                 wcooley at nakedape.cc
Naked Ape Consulting                        http://nakedape.cc
* * * * Linux, UNIX, Networking and Security Solutions * * * *

"Sufficiently advanced cluelessness is indistinguishable from malice." 
 -- Alex Martelli




From rlucas at tercent.com  Mon Dec 20 15:00:59 2004
From: rlucas at tercent.com (Randall Lucas)
Date: Mon, 20 Dec 2004 15:00:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] sftp and scp - preserving UID/GID
Message-ID: <41C759AB.3040306@tercent.com>


I believe scp -p preserves permissions.

 From man scp(1):

      -p      Preserves modification times, access times, and modes from 
the original file.

One caveat: if you have differing numeric UIDs GIDs between machines, I 
don't believe scp is smart enough to figure out username equivalences.

Randall

II/400MHz) from the same -- 1999 -- era). You can almost certainly find a
solid, used machine from a reputable seller that will meet your needs. On
the new end of the spectrum, "better deals" also depend on how you value
various options.

>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> I want to copy files from machine foo to machine bar, preserving their
> numeric UID/GID (user ID and group ID, for you newbies).  Assume I am
> doing this as root (on both ends), and the files have all sorts of
> UID/GID numbers, not necessarily matching the same users and groups
> for the two machines. 
> 
> Can this be done with sftp or scp?
> 
> The only way I know to do this now is with the -o option to tar, moving
> the files as a tar archive through an ssh pipe.  I am specifically
> interested in sftp or scp solutions if possible.

Likely you want the -p option to scp or -P for sftp get/put. Common
option for Preserve all permissions. Though this only covers the standard
permission bits, not the extended things like ACLs or ext2's immutable
flags.

On Mon, Dec 20, 2004 at 11:48:59AM -0800, Wil Cooley wrote:
> 
> I've got an older Wacom digitizer tablet that was made for Macs with an
> ADB connector (Apple Desktop Bus; it's the PS/2-like connectors used in
> pre-USB Macs for mice, keyboards, etc).  There are products available like
> the Griffin iMate (http://www.griffintechnology.com/products/imate/).  The
> problem, of course, is whether or not any of these devices have Linux
> support... The Linux USB HCL (http://linuxwacom.sourceforge.net/) turns up
> nothing for iMate (except "ulimate" and such) nor for ADB.

> of these things working?

Just speculating, but unless Griffin wanted to go out of their way to
make it difficult for themselves and require weird-ass drivers, the
iMate should just work like an ordinary USB HID.  And AFAIK, their
other products play nice with Linux.

Speaking of interesting USB HIDs, FreeGeek got in a pile of boards a
while ago.  They're for *serious* flight simulator fans, and are meant
to act like a bank of six jet engine throttle control levers.  They
look to Linux like a six-axis USB joystick.  I'm trying to see if I
can hack alternate inputs on 'em for sensing.

Subject: [PLUG] Re: sftp and scp - preserving UID/GID (2)
In-Reply-To: <20041221052714.GB11854@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20041221031517.660615AA8@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<20041221052714.GB11854@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <a4fbdc7b04122100204ee3b97c@mail.gmail.com>

On Mon, 20 Dec 2004 21:27:14 -0800, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
> 
> Randall Lucas writes:
> > I believe scp -p preserves permissions.
> 
> Russell Senior writes:
> > Er, why not rsync?  See the --numeric-ids option.
> 
> Elliott Mitchell writes:
> > Likely you want the -p option to scp or -P for sftp get/put.
> 
> Indeed, -p preserves permissions, but it does NOT preserve numeric
> UID/GID .  The reason I am asking is for a by-hand "move files one
> by one" situation, where the rsync command line is too much typing.
I believe scp cannot do what you want. Check this bug report:
http://bugzilla.mindrot.org/show_bug.cgi?id=427

There is a proposed patch to preserve uid/gid but it's rejected.

I suspect that there may be security ramifications as well with root
blindly creating files owned by another user. Definitely so if
permissions are fully preserved, including the setuid bit, but may be
even without it.

Anyway, the best way to copy everything exactly as-is is to pipe tar
through ssh, this also copies links accurately.

-- 
Fedor G Pikus (fpikus at gmail.com)
http://www.pikus.net
http://wild-light.com

On 21 Dec 2004 01:02:14 -0800, Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:
> >>>>> "Fedor" == Fedor Pikus <fpikus at gmail.com> writes:
> 
> Fedor> I suspect that there may be security ramifications as well with
> Fedor> root blindly creating files owned by another user.
> 
> Hey, if I have a root login on the box, piddly restrictions on what
> scp can do aren't going to save you (are they??).
No, the issue is not protecting the box from the root. It's protecting
the root from inadvertently compromizing his own system.

> Fedor> Anyway, the best way to copy everything exactly as-is is to
> Fedor> pipe tar through ssh, this also copies links accurately.
> 
> Is tar better in an observable way than rsync in this regard, and
> if so, how?
tar is also observable. rsync with -e ssh is about the same as tar
over ssh, although if you need to give ssh some options you have to
explicitly call it.

From publiustemp-plug at yahoo.com  Tue Dec 21 10:27:12 2004
From: publiustemp-plug at yahoo.com (Ovid)
Date: Tue, 21 Dec 2004 10:27:12 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] FC2 won't load some sites
Message-ID: <20041221182713.54099.qmail@web60801.mail.yahoo.com>

Hi all,

For some reason, FC2 2.6.8.1 does not seem to want to load some sites
in browsers.  I have this problem with Firefox, Mozilla, Konqueror,
etc.  One example is Perlmonks.org.  I can *sometimes* get to the site,
but never when I try to log in.  Typically I'll see the site title show
up in the tab and that's about it.

I can get there with Lynx, however.  The only information I've found
about this is at
http://www.fedoraforum.org/forum/archive/index.php/t-22833.html

The description of the problem is rather similar to mine, but due to a
problem with my graphics card driver, I can't boot into older kernel
versions and startx (as the person describing their problem did.)

There were suggestions that this was an IPV6 issue, so I tried adding
"IPV6INIT=no" to "/etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ifcfg-eth0".  I also
tried adding the following two lines to /etc/modprobe.conf:

  alias net-pf-10 off
  alias ipv6 off

Those didn't seem to help.  I'm stumped and I don't know if IPV6 is a
red herring or not.

I have this problem in both KDE and Fluxbox.

I also cannot get to easystreet.com or http://cornellsun.com, the site
that the author of the problem listed above mentioned.

Cheers,
Ovid

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Tue Dec 21 10:44:23 2004
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Tue, 21 Dec 2004 10:44:23 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: firefox extension management
References: <41C7CBDC.6090508@mpcu.com>
Message-ID: <pan.2004.12.21.18.44.23.201726@nakedape.cc>

On Mon, 20 Dec 2004 23:08:12 -0800, keith morse wrote:

> I've been looking for documentation on managing firefox's extensions. I'd
> like to install them once per system in order to have them available
>   for any subsequent users of the system.  So far my feeble attempts
> have not resulted in fruitfullness and the firefox website seems lacking
> in documentation in this concern.  Ideas?

Depending on how it was compiled, the system-wide directory for Mozilla
and Firefox plug-ins is /usr/lib/mozilla/plugins.  It might be easiest to
create this directory if it doesn't exist and chown it to your user, since
that's how you'll be running Firefox.

Wil

Ovid said:
> For some reason, FC2 2.6.8.1 does not seem to want to load some sites
> in browsers.  I have this problem with Firefox, Mozilla, Konqueror,
> etc.  One example is Perlmonks.org.  I can *sometimes* get to the site,
> but never when I try to log in.  Typically I'll see the site title show
> up in the tab and that's about it.

Sounds like a problem we had too when upgrading to FC2.  We could actually
get to the sites, but it took like seriously, many minutes.  Check out
this link and see if it pertains:

<http://lwn.net/Articles/92727/>

Something about the new kernel trying to use a large TCP segment/widow
size from default.  The article concludes that Internet routers along the
way are spontaneously rewriting the TCP header, which I totally doubt. 
However, the link above fixed our problem.

> I can get there with Lynx, however.

Hrm, that may be a fly in the ointment of my theory.  It seemed to be a
kernel issue, and wouldn't Lynx use the same kernel services as *zilla?

From creswick at gmail.com  Tue Dec 21 12:38:38 2004

Subject: [PLUG] Wally-world and the sub-$500 laptop..

On Mon, 20 Dec 2004 17:15:30 -0600, Tony Schlemmer <aschlemm at comcast.net> wrote:
> On Monday 20 December 2004 16:35, Chris Penwell wrote:
> > http://www.eweek.com/article2/0,1759,1744052,00.asp?kc=EWRSS03119TX1K000059
> >4
> >
> > Are there any better deals for someone who doesn't currently own a laptop?

Yes! Look at the IBM certified used equipment page (I'm sorry I can't
provide a link, but it's easy to find).  You can usually find ~1ghz
thinkpads in the 500-600 range. (with more memory, tested batteries..
IBM's name on the line, etc..)

Regarding the linspire box:

I second (or third, fourth?...) the memory concerns, but I'd also be
concerned about battery life.. they claim "over 1.5 hours.." In my
experience, anything less than 3-5 hours means you've got to carry a
power adapter everywhere you go, and be prepared to use it.  Keep in
mind that thrashing/swapping will be a significant hit to battery life
also--which brings us back to memory...

I'd also be concerned about weight, (8.0lbs shipping weight) you may
want to carry around an extra 8-lb book for a few days just to see
what it's like.   Depending on your useage patterns, this may not
matter, but it is a concern for many people.

-Rogan

Here's a true story about my thinkpad t20.  Anyone that has one (or a
t21, probably the same) just press on the lid a bit, you know, from
behind the screen, and watch the waves on your LCD.  There is pretty
much no protection there.  After taking mine to school and back for a
few months, I took it out of my bag one day and the LCD was shattered.
 I wasn't using a "laptop" bag, but after paying about $500 to get a
new LCD (actually used from ebay) installed I went and bought the most
expensive laptop bag with the most padding and protection I could
find.  I'm a little annoyed about that whole experience and wouldn't
wish it on anyone else.  I've also never found another laptop that has
a lid that  weak.

Bryan

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Tue Dec 21 23:31:19 2004
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)

Subject: [PLUG] Debian installation
In-Reply-To: <20041222065004.57122.qmail@web50610.mail.yahoo.com>

You shouldn't need any CD's.

Look for the stable floppies, or just burn the first CD....

I usually just use 6 floppies and an internet connection.

after you get an initial OS installed change your /etc/apt/sources.list
from
stable to testing.

that should get you KDE 3.3.

Or, use Knoppix to install debian.


Sean

From pem at nellump.net  Tue Dec 21 23:40:53 2004
From: pem at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Tue, 21 Dec 2004 23:40:53 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Debian installation
In-Reply-To: <20041222065004.57122.qmail@web50610.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20041222065004.57122.qmail@web50610.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20041222074053.GA5445@nellump.net>

On Tue, Dec 21, 2004 at 10:50:04PM -0800, Robert Kopp wrote:
> Many people on this list use Debian, so maybe I should
> try it. There are 7 CD's for each architecture, so I
> suppose I should get all seven. With a fast
> connection, that isn't too much of a problem.

If you have a fast connection, don't bother downloading a boatload of
ISOs. Just download one of the net-install ISOs and install everything
else via one of the package repositories. That's one of Debian's finer
points. There really is no need to think in terms discrete "releases"
when you're only an "apt-get upgrade" away from the latest packages.

> "Woody" is said to include KDE 2.2. That must be a
> joke. If they're serious, I suppose there's some way
> to upgrade easily to 3.x?

Last I looked "Sid" offered KDE 3.3. Don't bother with Woody (AKA
"stable") unless you're really paranoid about security and stability
(*really* paranoid). Stick to Sid (AKA "unstable", and anything but)
if it's a normal user machine (workstation, laptop, etc.).

NB: In my experience, the Debian maintainers use "stable" and
"unstable" in a different way than one would normally expect in the
context of personal computers. They don't mean that Sid is unstable in
the sense that your computer will be crashing a lot. Rather, it means
that the package tree is in flux, and that individual packages can and
do change quite often (meaning you may want to upgrade every week or
so).


Paul

I vote for what everyone else said. I run Sid (unstable) on a lot of machines 
and it works just fine. The trouble spots are with new releases, especially 
big complex packages like KDE and Gnome. Sometimes it takes a couple of weeks  
to work out all the dependencies. The system is not unstable; the "unstable" 
bit is working out the dependency kinks.

Debian probably has the smoothest upgrade path of any Linux. Install once for 
life.

Getting used to apt-get can take a bit of work, this might help:
http://www.debian.org/doc/

From rsteff.l at comcast.net  Wed Dec 22 10:32:05 2004
From: rsteff.l at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Wed, 22 Dec 2004 10:32:05 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
Message-ID: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>

I'm finally getting around to moving /home over to the new partition I created 
last week (or was it the week before?). I read info coreutils cp, but got it 
wrong, so I'm looking for the right way to move the files and preserve all 
the attributes.

I have the new partition mounted on /home2. As root, I tried copying thus:

cp -r /home /home2

This resulted in a copy of home in /home2. In other words, /home2 had in it, 
home. Also, all the files ended up with root as the owner.

Re-reading info coreutils cp, I discovered that I should have use the -p 
option to preserve the attributes. However it's still a little fuzzy, so 
here's today's question: is this the form of the cp command I should have 
used?

cp -r -p all /home/rsteff /home2

(The only directory in /home is rsteff.)

Or, is there another command I should be using?

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Dec 22 10:37:34 2004
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 22 Dec 2004 10:37:34 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.60.0412221035420.13018@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 22 Dec 2004, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> cp -r -p all /home/rsteff /home2

Dick,

   What I've done is:

 	cp -Ra /home/rsteff/* /home2

Lowercase 'r' is usually a synonym for uppercase 'R'. The 'a' is for
archive:

        -a, --archive
               Preserve as much as possible of the  structure  and
               attributes  of  the original files in the copy (but
               do not preserve directory  structure).   Equivalent
               to -dpPR.

HTH,

Rich

<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>   Voice: 503-667-4517   Fax: 503-667-8863


From rich at paranoid.org  Wed Dec 22 10:39:52 2004
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Wed, 22 Dec 2004 10:39:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <41C9BF78.2000609@paranoid.org>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Re-reading info coreutils cp, I discovered that I should have use the -p 
> option to preserve the attributes. However it's still a little fuzzy, so 
> here's today's question: is this the form of the cp command I should have 
> used?
> 
> cp -r -p all /home/rsteff /home2
> 
> (The only directory in /home is rsteff.)
> 
> Or, is there another command I should be using?

Hi Richard,

Sounds like you've seen this, but here's a man page:

http://www.zevils.com/cgi-bin/man/man2html?1+cp

For the kind of thing you're doing, I'd usually use the -a option, which 
is the same as -dpR.

Try:

   cp -a /home/rsteff /home2

Another option would be to use tar if you're more comfortable with that. 
In /home do:

   tar cvf /home2/rsteff.tar rsteff

That will create a tarball in the /home2 directory that has the rsteff 
directory in it.

   cd /home2
   tar xvf rsteff.tar

You might do "tar tvf rsteff.tar" first, to make sure that what you're 
extracting looks right and is in the right place.


Rich

p.s. I'm very tired right now, so be careful using my suggestions ;)

is the closest you  get with cp, but it does not always get links right.
The best way is with tar:
cd /home
tar cf - . | (cd ../home2; tar xvf - )

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Dec 22 10:48:12 2004
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 22 Dec 2004 10:48:12 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <41C9BF78.2000609@paranoid.org>
References: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
	<41C9BF78.2000609@paranoid.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0412221044210.2498@pecos.madboa.com>

On Wed, 22 Dec 2004, Rich Burroughs wrote:

> Try:
>
>  cp -a /home/rsteff /home2
>
> Another option would be to use tar if you're more comfortable with 
> that. In /home do:
>
>  tar cvf /home2/rsteff.tar rsteff
>  cd /home2
>  tar xvf rsteff.tar

Perhaps a bit cleaner would be

   tar -c -C /home . | tar -x -C /home2

Note: this recipe relies on some switches and defaults unique to GNU 
tar. More portable would be

   cd /home
   tar cf - . | (cd /home2 && tar xf -)

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>


From seniorr at aracnet.com  Wed Dec 22 10:59:57 2004
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 22 Dec 2004 10:59:57 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <864qief9aq.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Richard" == Richard C Steffens <rsteff.l at comcast.net> writes:

Richard> I'm finally getting around to moving /home over to the new
Richard> partition I created last week (or was it the week before?). I
Richard> read info coreutils cp, but got it wrong, so I'm looking for
Richard> the right way to move the files and preserve all the
Richard> attributes.

[...]

Richard> Or, is there another command I should be using?

I'd use either:

  rsync -v -a -H /home/ /home2/

or 

  tar -C /home -c . | tar -C /home2 -xpv

A few things to note:

  a) with this rsync command any files that were in /home2 before the
  command but that don't exist in /home will remain.  If you want an
  exact up-to-date copy of /home in /home2, you probably want to use
  the --delete option.

  b) the trailing slashes are significant in rsync.  I've forgotten
  exactly what the significance is, but the above example should
  work.  Read the man page for details.

  c) with the tar-to-tar, it is important *not* to include the verbose
  option on the first tar.  You are depending on standard input/output
  to move your data.  It is not likely to be pretty if you include the
  verbose information in that stream.

  d) there are many variations on the tar-to-tar technique.  If you
  have gnu tar at both ends, then this variant will work.

  e) both methods can work over a network through an ssh tunnel by
  appropriate modification, e.g.:

    rsync -e ssh -v -a -H /home/ hostname:/home2/

  or

    tar -C /home -c . | ssh hostname 'tar -C /home2 -xpv'


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
seniorr at aracnet.com

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

>I'm finally getting around to moving /home over to the new partition I created 
>last week (or was it the week before?). I read info coreutils cp, but got it 
>wrong, so I'm looking for the right way to move the files and preserve all 
>the attributes.
>
>I have the new partition mounted on /home2. As root, I tried copying thus:
>
>cp -r /home /home2
>  
>
And as per usual, there's a bunch of ways to do it.

I usually use this command:

root@/home $ find . | cpio -pdm /home2

When it's done, cpio had duplicated the contents of /home in /home2.

Russ

From ehem at m5p.com  Wed Dec 22 12:09:37 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Wed, 22 Dec 2004 12:09:37 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <20041222192433.5DD515AA8@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200412222009.iBMK9bqN060639@m5p.com>

>From: Rich Burroughs <rich at paranoid.org>
> Fedor Pikus wrote:
> 
> > The best way is with tar:
> 
> Yeah, I agree, tar is definitely better for this sort of thing. It will 
> preserve everything.

I might be the odd one out in suggesting it, but this sounds like the
size problem where I prefer dump. This is because dump really *can*
preserve everything. Particularly with home directories since folks can
do interesting things...

ls -l (slightly edited)
total 8676
-rw-r--r--   4 ehem      1099511627775 Aug  4 14:02 -f
-rw-r--r--   4 ehem      1099511627775 Aug  4 14:02 -fr
-rw-r--r--   4 ehem      1099511627775 Aug  4 14:02 -r
-rw-r--r--   4 ehem      1099511627775 Aug  4 14:02 -rf
drwxr-s--x  28 ehem         3072 Feb 17  2004 CAT
drwxr-xr-x   2 ehem          512 Mar 21  2004 LL
drwx------   2 ehem          512 Mar 17  2001 Mail
drwxr-xr-x   2 ehem          512 Jul  3  2000 RCS
drwxr-xr-x   2 ehem          512 Apr 13  2003 bin
drwxr-xr-x   3 ehem          512 Sep 19  2001 include
drwxr-xr-x   2 ehem          512 Sep 19  2001 lib
drwxr-xr-x  20 ehem          512 Apr 22  1999 man
drwxr-xr-x  15 ehem          512 Aug  4 13:58 src
drwx------   9 ehem          512 Sep 11 14:30 stuff
drwxr-xr-x   2 ehem          512 Aug  4 13:59 threads

Yes, those are four 1TB files there with interesting named. Depending on
the flavor of tar, it could take hours to dump this, and it might well
fail to fit onto the target FS. tar might also fail to notice that those
four are hard links to each other.

So for dump/restore:
dump -0f - | ( cd <FS> ; restore -xf - )

And this *will* preserve interesting files like the above.

I'll add my voice to the dump|restore option.  IIRC, it'll handle 
"sparse" files
better than tar.  Sparse files have large blocks of 0's which aren't really
allocated as disk blocks. (I don't know how the filesystem tracks such 
stuff,
or if such is even supported in ext2/ext3.)

I remember core files on some Sun systems claiming to occupy 100's of MB
of disk, but after deletion, df would only show 10's of MB freed.  No biggie
until you try copying some users home dirs using tar, and they suddenly
blow up 10x in size!  tar-based backup programs would have problems with
this, too.

>filesystems are different?

Yes -- they are really meant to work on complete filesystems at a time,
not just directories/folders.  Safest results are if the source 
filesystem is
unmounted or mounted read-only.

If you've ever had to restore items from a tape with only a vague idea of
the filename, the interactive restore program ("restore -i") is much easier
than reading the whole tape with "tar t" to get the table of contents.



From seniorr at aracnet.com  Wed Dec 22 16:00:00 2004
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 22 Dec 2004 16:00:00 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <41CA0479.1020901@easystreet.com>
References: <200412222009.iBMK9bqN060639@m5p.com>
	<41CA0479.1020901@easystreet.com>
Message-ID: <86hdmdeven.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Don" == Don Buchholz <buchholz at easystreet.com> writes:

Don> I'll add my voice to the dump|restore option.  IIRC, it'll handle
Don> "sparse" files better than tar.  Sparse files have large blocks
Don> of 0's which aren't really allocated as disk blocks. (I don't
Don> know how the filesystem tracks such stuff, or if such is even
Don> supported in ext2/ext3.)

Yes, ext2/3 do sparse files.

Don> I remember core files on some Sun systems claiming to occupy
Don> 100's of MB of disk, but after deletion, df would only show 10's
Don> of MB freed.  No biggie until you try copying some users home
Don> dirs using tar, and they suddenly blow up 10x in size!  tar-based
Don> backup programs would have problems with this, too.

GNU tar with -S creates sparse-honoring archives.  For example, try:

  $ dd if=/dev/zero of=i-am-sparse count=1 seek=1023
  $ ls -ls i-am-sparse
  $ ln i-am-sparse i-am-spars2
  $ ln i-am-sparse i-am-spars3
  $ ln i-am-sparse i-am-spars4
  $ tar -cSf /tmp/sparse.tar i-am-spars[e234]
  $ pushd /tmp
  $ tar xvf sparse.tar
  $ ls -ls i-am-spars[e234]


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
seniorr at aracnet.com


From pem at nellump.net  Wed Dec 22 15:58:21 2004

On Wed, Dec 22, 2004 at 12:17:55PM -0800, Bryan Murdock wrote:

> Does it preserve tweaks to stuff in /etc/whatever well?  I have a
> Mandrake 10 machine at home that is set-up just perfectly, and I've
> been afraid to upgrade it because I know I'll have to redo all the
> little tweaks that I've made.  It'd be really cool to never have to
> fear that again.  Probably worth a distro switch.

Strict policy is one of the things I like about Debian. I find it
tremenously irritating when an package installer doesn't respect the
changes I've made to by own system. When a Debian package update
contains a new version of a config file, and the installer notices
you've made changes to the current config, it'll give you the option
of installing the new version or keeping your old one. In either case,
the version you don't use will be saved with an appropriately obvious
file name extension so that you can go in and merge the differences by
hand.


Paul

From ehem at m5p.com  Wed Dec 22 19:05:30 2004
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Wed, 22 Dec 2004 19:05:30 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <20041223012224.97C395AA2@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200412230305.iBN35UeN065046@m5p.com>

>From: Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com>
> >>>>> "Elliott" == Elliott Mitchell <ehem at m5p.com> writes:
> 
> Elliott> Yes, those are four 1TB files there with interesting
> Elliott> named. Depending on the flavor of tar, it could take hours to
> Elliott> dump this, and it might well fail to fit onto the target
> Elliott> FS. tar might also fail to notice that those four are hard
> Elliott> links to each other.
> 
> GNU tar handles hardlinks and (with -S) sparse files.
> 
> Curious, will dump-restore work if you source and destination
> filesystems are different?

Yes. dump is filesystem specific, restore is not. If the destination
filesystem doesn't support features that the source filesystem does
though, you'll lose those features but otherwise everything works.

Things like permissions, hard links and sparse files have a well defined
API through the kernel and will always be preserved. Extended ACLs and
flags (such as ext2's nodump, immutable, append-only, etc) are only
guarenteed on the same type of filesystem. Large files may also be
truncated if the target doesn't support files that large.

>From: Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com>
> >>>>> "Don" == Don Buchholz <buchholz at easystreet.com> writes:
> 
> Don> I remember core files on some Sun systems claiming to occupy
> Don> 100's of MB of disk, but after deletion, df would only show 10's
> Don> of MB freed.  No biggie until you try copying some users home
> Don> dirs using tar, and they suddenly blow up 10x in size!  tar-based
> Don> backup programs would have problems with this, too.
> 
> GNU tar with -S creates sparse-honoring archives.  For example, try:
> 
>   $ dd if=/dev/zero of=i-am-sparse count=1 seek=1023
>   $ ls -ls i-am-sparse
>   $ ln i-am-sparse i-am-spars2
>   $ ln i-am-sparse i-am-spars3
>   $ ln i-am-sparse i-am-spars4
>   $ tar -cSf /tmp/sparse.tar i-am-spars[e234]
>   $ pushd /tmp
>   $ tar xvf sparse.tar
>   $ ls -ls i-am-spars[e234]

It will still need to read a terabyte of zeros though.

From jason at vancleve.com  Thu Dec 23 11:32:00 2004
From: jason at vancleve.com (Jason Van Cleve)
Date: Thu, 23 Dec 2004 11:32:00 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Proper way to copy directories
In-Reply-To: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
References: <200412221032.05315.rsteff.l@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20041223113200.5b3eb350.jason@vancleve.com>

Quoth Richard C. Steffens, on Wed, 22 Dec 2004 10:32:05 -0800:

> cp -r /home /home2
> 
> This resulted in a copy of home in /home2. In other words, /home2 had

With all us experts on file copy techniques, you might feel silly for
trying to use a tool so primitive as cp.  But after all, it's probably
all you need.

I wanted to point out that you probably had an existing /home2 directory
when you ran the cp command above:  that would explain why /home ended
up within /home2.  When copying one directory path to another, using cp,
what happens depends on whether the destination directory already
exists.  If it does NOT exist, then cp will create it as a direct copy
of the source directory.

Thus, if you remove /home2 completely and then execute

cp -pR /home /home2

you should get what you bargained for.  Works for a one-off, but if
you're doing a backup and plan to repeat it, you should use a more
suitable tool.

--Jason

User has performed an invalid operation and will be terminated.

From baloo at ursine.dyndns.org  Thu Dec 23 12:14:11 2004
From: baloo at ursine.dyndns.org (Paul Johnson)
Date: Thu, 23 Dec 2004 12:14:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Software News from Europe & Detroit
In-Reply-To: <41CAA8D9.4060303@aracnet.com>
References: <200412221645.iBMGjtMD020156@jade.spiritone.com>
	<477eba8c041222122054d43e25@mail.gmail.com>
	<41CAA8D9.4060303@aracnet.com>
Message-ID: <200412231214.17171.baloo@ursine.dyndns.org>

On Thursday 23 December 2004 3:15 am, techmage at aracnet.com wrote:

> I think the best reason is that the most recent distros have conquered 
> my dread of setting up PPP for dial up. In my last three installs, it 
> has worked flawlessly from the start. I'm still working on the " How 
> to do dialup without logging in as root" thing. But, for now, I'm 
> accepting that one little flaw.  

In Debian, the easiest way to do it in the long term from what I've 
found requires an external modem with a power switch.  Use pppconfig to 
set up your connection and tell it to persist and to automatically 
reconnect if it disconnects.  mv /etc/ppp/no_ppp_on_boot 
to /etc/ppp/ppp_on_boot.  PPP is now set up to try to reconnect at all 
costs.  Use the switch on the modem to turn on or off the connection.  
It may take a few seconds to dialup again when you turn on the modem.

Simple, elegant and it works.  What more could you ask for?

-- 
Paul Johnson
baloo at ursine.dyndns.org
http://ursine.dyndns.org/~baloo/

ClamAV is pretty nice.  It does miss, however, a number of trojans.  (I 
have a source for backdoors and other nasty viruses (called Usenet) that I 
test with a couple of virus scanners.  Anything that ClamAV misses gets 
submitted to their online posting area.)

One other problem with ClamAV is that it will mistake multipart archives 
as being "oversized" on occasion.  (Enough of a false positive ratio that 
I have to disable that set of tests.)

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Thu Dec 23 19:35:48 2004
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
